Third Argument: Revelation Prevents Social Chaos
The third argument: revelation prevents social chaos and destruction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's third argument: without divine law, competition for survival would lead to mutual destruction and extinction.

Core Idea
Without revelation, natural desires lead to deadly conflict.

In his third argument, al-Nasafi examines the social dimension. Humans naturally love survival and desire resources. Without a divinely legislated law (sharʿ) to set boundaries and allocate rights, everyone would seize whatever they think ensures their survival, leading to violent conflict, killing, and eventual extinction of the species. Such an outcome is foolish and unworthy of a wise Creator. Therefore, revelation is necessary to prevent chaos.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and note the causal chain: no law → competition → conflict → mutual annihilation.
2
Identify the role of 'sharʿ'
Understand that 'sharʿ' (divine law) is presented as the solution to prevent this destructive cycle.
3
Consider the additional example
Notice the mention of desirable worldly goods like slave girls (al-jawārī) as a source of conflict. This reinforces the argument.

المقصود من الوقوف على ذلك لا يحصل إلا بالتجربة إذ ليس كل سم يقتل من ساعته، ولا كل مضر يظهر أثره عقيب تناوله، إذ من السموم ما يظهر أثره ويتفاقم ضرره بعد أسبوع، ومنه ما يظهر ذلك بعد شهر وبعد سنة، معروف ذلك عند المختصين بعلم جواهر الأشياء وطبائع الموجودات، ولو أمر غيره ممن تحت تصرفه بتناول ذلك يتناول هو بنفسه ذلك ليدفع به عن نفسه ألم الجوع وضرر العطش ويحصل له به ما هو محبوبة من البقاء إذ لم ير أثر ضرر ظهر عن تناوله لأدى ذلك عسى إلى تلف مهجته وفساد بنيته بعد ذلك بزمان لما كان ذلك المتناول مما يضر بعد شهر أو سنة، ولو أمسك عن تناول الأغذية منتظرا ظهور أثره إلى شهر أو سنة لمات جوعاً وعطشاً قبل بلوغ ما ينتظره من المدة. ثم بعد هذا نقرر الكلام من وجوه أحدها أن الصانع جل وعلا لو لم يبين الأغذية من الأدوية والسموم، ولم يتجاسر العقلاء على التجربة، ولم يتوصلوا إلى الانتفاع بالأغذية، واستدامة مهجتهم، لم يكن لخلق الأغذية التي هي ما عليه العادة الجارية أسباب البقاء حكمة ويحصل إيجادها على العبث والسفه جل الله عن ذلك. والثاني: أن أبدان الممتحنين لو لم يجعل لهم الوصول إلى ما هو من أسباب بقائها تلفت بأوهى مدة وأسرع زمان من غير حصول معنى لإيجادهم سوى الفناء، وخلق الخلق للفناء خاصة من غير عاقبة حميدة تتعلق به مستقبح في العقول خارج عن الحكمة فلا بد من البيان الوارد من منشئ الأشياء على طبائعها المختلفة، العالم بجواهرها وما يتعلق بها من الحكمة على لسان ما شاء من عباده، ليخرج تخليق الجواهر التي هي من أسباب البقاء وأحداث الأبدان المحتاجة في دوامها وبقائها إلى الأغذية المجعولة أسبابًا لذلك عن السفه ويدخل في حيز ما هو حكمة. والثالث: أن بعد وقوفهم على ما يتعلق به بقاء أبدانهم، ولا شك أن كلا جبلوا على حب البقاء وطلب ما يحصل لهم به الدوام، فلو لم يشرع لهم الحكيم شرعًا يؤمرون بالتمسك به والانتهاء إلى حدوده مشتملاً على أسباب تبنى عليها الأحكام ويعرف بها اختصاص كل من اختص بشيء منها بما هو حكمه. وينقطع طمع من لاحظ له من تلك الأسباب عن الفوز بتلك الأحكام لتسارع كل إلى ما عرف به بقاءه ولتناول ما يرجو فيه دوامه، فيقع حينئذ بينهم أقبح تجاذب وأقطع تغالب، ثم يفضي ذلك إلى التقاتل، وذلك مما تؤول عاقبته إلى التفاني وانقطاع النسل وارتفاع الجنس وانعدام عاقبة لإيجاد الممتحنين سوى الفناء خاصة وذلك سفه. وكذا لا شك أن في أجزاء العالم ما كانت الطبائع إليه مائلة والرغبات فيه ممتدة والنفوس إلى الاستيلاء عليه والاختصاص بالاستمتاع به متسارعة، والقلوب بنبله والظفر به والوصول إلى الارتفاق به منشرحة مرتاحة من نحو الجواري
regarding how some differ from others, and not knowing what necessitates differentiation between each of that; and the intellect cannot undertake experimentation itself because of the risk of destruction, nor with those under its authority because it would expose others to death or loss. Yet the intended goal of ascertaining that is not attained except through experimentation, since not every poison kills instantly, nor does every harmful thing show its effect immediately after consumption. Among poisons are those whose effect appears and whose harm intensifies after a week, and among them are those whose effect appears after a month or a year—well-known to specialists in the science of the essences of things and the natures of existents. And if one orders another under his authority to consume that, then he himself consumes it to ward off from himself the pain of hunger and the harm of thirst and to obtain what is desired of survival, since he saw no harmful effect from its consumption, that would perhaps lead to the destruction of his soul and the corruption of his constitution after some time, because that consumed substance harms after a month or a year. Creation Without Revelation Is Foolishness
Second argument: creation would be futile without revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second kalam argument for the necessity of revelation: that without prophetic guidance, creation would be futile and irrational.

Core Idea
Without revelation, creation would be an act of foolishness, not wisdom.

This lesson presents a powerful rational argument for why divine revelation is not optional but necessary. The argument is based on a simple observation: if creation had no purpose beyond temporary existence and annihilation, it would be senseless—something unworthy of a wise Creator. Therefore, God must have sent revelation to give creation meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the conditional structure and the conclusion.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Find where the author states that if creation were only for annihilation, it would be repugnant to intellects and outside wisdom.
This is the key logical move.
3
Extract the conclusion
See that the conclusion is the necessity of a statement (bayan) from the Creator to remove foolishness.
The phrase 'فلا بد من البيان' signals the conclusion.
4
Reflect on key terms
Consider 'safah' (foolishness) and 'hikma' (wisdom) - they are opposites in this argument.
These terms anchor the moral and rational force of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقائها تلفت بأوهى مدة وأسرع زمان من غير حصول معنى لإيجادهم سوى الفناء، وخلق الخلق للفناء خاصة من غير عاقبة حميدة تتعلق به مستقبح في العقول خارج عن الحكمة فلا بد من البيان الوارد من منشئ الأشياء على طبائعها المختلفة، العالم بجواهرها وما يتعلق بها من الحكمة على لسان ما شاء من عباده، ليخرج تخليق الجواهر التي هي من أسباب البقاء وأحداث الأبدان المحتاجة في دوامها وبقائها إلى الأغذية المجعولة أسبابًا لذلك عن السفه ويدخل في حيز ما هو حكمة.
Their existence perishes in the weakest duration and fastest time, with no meaning for their creation except annihilation. And creating creation for annihilation alone, without a praiseworthy consequence attached to it, is considered repugnant by intellects, outside of wisdom. So there must be a statement sent from the Originator of things upon their different natures, the Knower of their essences and what pertains to them of wisdom, upon the tongue of whomever He wills of His servants, to remove the creation of essences which are among the causes of permanence, and the generation of bodies needing for their continuity and permanence the foods made causes for that, from foolishness, and to enter it into the realm of wisdom.

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the necessity of divine revelation. It begins by observing that if creation had no purpose beyond annihilation, that would be foolishness (safah) and outside wisdom (hikma). Therefore, there must be a revelation (bayan) from the Creator, who knows the essences of things and what wisdom pertains to them. This revelation is necessary to remove the creation of essences and bodies, which are sustained by food, from the charge of foolishness and to place it within the realm of wisdom.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument only concerns the need for food to sustain bodies.
Correction: The argument actually addresses the entire purpose of creation—without revelation, existence itself would be futile and foolish.

B) It would be considered foolishness by intellects

D) It would be inevitable

Key Takeaway
Revelation is necessary because without it, creation would be purposeless and irrational, contradicting divine wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If someone asked you why you follow revelation, could you say, 'Because without it, I would be a fool'? How does this truth reshape your commitment to learning and obeying what God has sent?

Third Argument: Human Nature and Necessity of Law
Third argument: without law, human desires lead to destruction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for the necessity of divine law based on human nature's love of permanence and the inevitable chaos without a lawgiver.

Core Idea
Without divine law, human desires would lead to mutual destruction.

This passage presents the third argument from the Maturidi tradition for the necessity of prophethood and divine law. It builds on human nature's love of permanence and the natural competition for resources. The argument shows that without a lawgiver to define entitlements, society would descend into conflict and annihilation, which is irrational and unworthy of the Wise Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the two main parts: the argument from survival and the argument from worldly attractions.
2
Identify the two main components
First, the argument focuses on humans' natural love of life and how without law they would fight for survival. Second, it extends to all desirable things like wealth and beauty. Identify these two parts.
The passage uses examples like slave-girls, horses, and gardens.
3
Understand the consequences
Without a law regulating entitlements, the argument says conflict leads to mutual annihilation, cessation of progeny, and ultimately the attribution of foolishness to God. Reflect on why this is considered irrational.
4
Grasp the necessity of shar'
The passage concludes that a law is necessary to assign ownership through legitimate causes, preventing disputes and maintaining order. It ends with a condemnation of those who deny the law.
The term 'shar'' refers to divine law.

والثالث: أن بعد وقوفهم على ما يتعلق به بقاء أبدانهم، ولا شك أن كلا جبلوا على حب البقاء وطلب ما يحصل لهم به الدوام، فلو لم يشرع لهم الحكيم شرعًا يؤمرون بالتمسك به والانتهاء إلى حدوده مشتملاً على أسباب تبنى عليها الأحكام ويعرف بها اختصاص كل من اختص بشيء منها بما هو حكمه. وينقطع طمع من لاحظ له من تلك الأسباب عن الفوز بتلك الأحكام لتسارع كل إلى ما عرف به بقاءه ولتناول ما يرجو فيه دوامه، فيقع حينئذ بينهم أقبح تجاذب وأقطع تغالب، ثم يفضي ذلك إلى التقاتل، وذلك مما تؤول عاقبته إلى التفاني وانقطاع النسل وارتفاع الجنس وانعدام عاقبة لإيجاد الممتحنين سوى الفناء خاصة وذلك سفه. وكذا لا شك أن في أجزاء العالم ما كانت الطبائع إليه مائلة والرغبات فيه ممتدة والنفوس إلى الاستيلاء عليه والاختصاص بالاستمتاع به متسارعة، والقلوب بنبله والظفر به والوصول إلى الارتفاق به منشرحة مرتاحة من نحو الجواري الحسان والغلمان الموصوفين بالجمال والدواب الفارهة، والدور الواسعة، والبساتين النزهة والأمتعة الفاخرة. فلولا الشيء الموجب اختصاص من اختص بسبب الملك لها لامتدت الأيدي الخاطفة الخاطئة والأطماع الفاسدة إلى كل من ذلك ولوقعت بينهم المنازعة المولدة للعداوة والبغضاء المورثة للضغائن والأحقاد، وكل ذلك مما يحمل على القتال وهو يؤدي إلى ما بيننا من انقطاع النسل وارتفاع الجنس المقصود بتخليق العالم، وذلك يوجب أن خلق الخلق لم يكن إلا للفناء خاصة وهو سفه جل ربنا عن ذلك وتعالى. التملك فإذا لا بد من شرع يبين اختصاص كل من البشر بما له من أصناف النعم بسبب يمتاز به عن غيره، ويحظر على من سواه ممن لا حظ له من سبب لتلك الأشياء منازعة أربابها المختصين بها لاختصاصهم بأسباب توجب لهم الملك فيها ليندفع الجدال ويرتفع الخصام، ويبقى العالم على ما له من الترتيب والنظام. فمن أنكر الشرع وأبطل الأمر والنهي فقد سعى في إثارة العبث والفساد في العالم، وإثبات أسباب التقاتل والتفاني، ونسب صانع العالم إلى السفه والجهل تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا.
And the third: that after their coming to know what pertains to the permanence of their bodies, and there is no doubt that each person is naturally disposed to love of permanence and seeking what brings them continuity, so if the Wise One had not prescribed for them a law commanding them to adhere to it and abide by its limits, comprising causes upon which rulings are built and by which the entitlement of each who is entitled to something of them is known according to his ruling, and the hope of those who have no share of those causes is cut off from attaining those rulings, because each hurries to that by which his permanence is known and to take that in which he hopes for his continuity, then there occurs among them the ugliest tugging and most decisive overpowering, then that leads to fighting, and that results in mutual annihilation, cessation of progeny, disappearance of the species, and no consequence for the creation of the tested ones except annihilation, and that is foolishness. And likewise there is no doubt that among the parts of the world are things toward which natures incline, desires stretch, souls race to seize and exclusively enjoy, and hearts are delighted and at ease with its excellence, attainment, and benefitting, such as beautiful slave-girls, handsome boys, fine horses, spacious houses, pleasant gardens, and luxurious goods. Were it not for something that necessitates the entitlement of one who is entitled due to a cause of ownership, then grasping wrong hands and corrupt ambitions would stretch to all of that, and disputes would arise generating enmity and hatred, giving rise to grudges and rancor. All that leads to fighting, which leads to what we mentioned: cessation of progeny and disappearance of the species intended by the creation of the world. That necessitates that the creation of creatures was only for annihilation, which is foolishness—glory be to our Lord, and exalted is He above that. Ownership: so there must be a law that clarifies the entitlement of each human to what he has of various blessings due to a cause by which he is distinguished from others, and prohibits others who have no share in a cause for those things from disputing the owners entitled to them due to their having causes that give them ownership in them, so that argument and dispute are repelled, and the world remains in its due order and system. Whoever denies the law and invalidates command and prohibition has striven to excite frivolity and corruption in the world, to establish causes of fighting and mutual annihilation, and has attributed foolishness and ignorance to the Maker of the world—exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the necessity of divine law (shar'). It begins by noting that humans are naturally inclined to love of life and seeking permanence. Without a prescribed law from the Wise One to regulate entitlements and set limits, people would rush to secure their own survival, leading to conflict, fighting, and ultimately mutual annihilation—which is foolishness. The argument then extends to worldly attractions (beautiful women, handsome boys, fine horses, spacious houses, gardens, luxury goods). Without a cause-based entitlement system (such as ownership), people's greedy hands would seize everything, generating disputes, enmity, and hatred, again leading to destruction. Thus, a law is necessary to assign ownership through legitimate causes, thereby preventing conflict and maintaining order. The passage concludes by condemning those who deny the law as promoting frivolity and corruption, and attributing foolishness to God.

تملك

نسل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only applies to material goods like wealth and property.
Correction: The argument covers all human desires, including love of life itself, and applies to all aspects of social harmony.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, what is the ultimate consequence of denying the law?
A) Social progress
B) Mutual annihilation and attribution of foolishness to God
C) Greater individual freedom
D) Natural balance

Key Takeaway
The third argument demonstrates that divine law is essential to prevent chaos and preserve human society, reflecting the wisdom of the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author says that without divine law, creation would be futile and foolish. Do you live as though your life has a purpose beyond your own survival and pleasure? If not, what would it take for you to start living in alignment with that purpose today?
?
This passage argues that denying the law 'excites frivolity and corruption.' In what areas of your life are you tempted to think that God's commands are unnecessary or restrictive? How can you actively recommit to following His law, even when it is hard?

Rational Person Rejects Indulgence in Desires
Reflection on the rational rejection of indulgence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a rational person naturally rejects indulgence in desires and neglect of intellect, which points to the need for divine guidance.

Core Idea
Rationality itself demands using the intellect for higher pursuits.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi concludes a rational reflection by stating that no rational person would be pleased with giving in to desires and neglecting the intellect. It shows that the very nature of rationality (aql) impels a person toward acquiring knowledge and avoiding corruption, which aligns with the purpose of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the tone: it is a definitive rational statement.
The passage is a single complex sentence; pay attention to the list of negative behaviors.
2
Identify the negative behaviors listed
The passage lists several things no rational person would be pleased with. Find them: indulging in desires, giving the soul everything it wishes, immoralities, frivolity, corruption, oppressing others, and neglecting the intellect.
Each item reinforces the point that rationality opposes these actions.
3
Reflect on the positive alternative
Contrast the negative with what the intellect should be used for: reflecting on realities and acquiring sciences. Consider how this aligns with the purpose of prophethood.
The passage ends mid-sentence but the implication is clear: the rational person pursues knowledge and righteousness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه لا عاقل في الشاهد يرضى من نفسه الانهماك في الشهوات وإعطاء النفس الأمارة بالسوء جميع ما تتمنى من الفواحش وتهوى من القبائح والاشتغال بالعبث والفساد، وظلم من سواه من العباد وإهمال العقل الذي هو من واهب الله الجزيلة ومنائحة الجليلة عن التفكر في حقائق الأشياء، واكتساب العلوم التي بإحرازها واقتنائها تشرف النفوس وتعظم الأخطار ويجل في أعين الخلق،
And furthermore, no rational person in the witnessed world would be pleased with himself for indulging in desires, giving the soul that commands evil everything it wishes of immoralities and covets of abominations, occupying himself with frivolity and corruption, oppressing others among the servants, neglecting the intellect—which is from God's abundant gift and noble benefaction—from reflecting on the realities of things, and acquiring sciences by whose attainment and possession souls are ennobled, dangers are magnified, and one becomes exalted in the eyes of people.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a reflective conclusion that reinforces the necessity of divine law from a rational perspective. It states that no rational person (aql) in the observable world (shahid) would be pleased with himself for engaging in a list of negative behaviors: indulging in desires, obeying the inciting soul, committing immoralities, engaging in frivolity and corruption, oppressing others, and neglecting the intellect (aql) which is a great gift from God. The intellect should be used for reflecting on the realities of things and acquiring sciences (ulum) that ennoble the soul and bring honor. Thus, a person of reason would naturally avoid these faults and seek knowledge and righteousness, which aligns with the purpose of prophethood and law.

Misconception: This is merely moral advice.
Correction: It is a rational proof for the need for divine guidance to perfect the intellect.

B) Reflecting on realities
C) Neglecting the intellect
D) Using the intellect

Key Takeaway
The use of intellect for reflection and knowledge acquisition is a natural demand of rationality, which prophethood perfects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your daily life, are you more often pleasing your lower soul (nafs ammārah) by indulging in desires, or are you actively using your intellect (ʿaql) to reflect on the deeper realities of your existence—your purpose, your relationship with God, and your accountability? What is one specific change you can make today to shift the balance?
?
The passage states that no rational person would be pleased with himself for oppressing others or engaging in frivolity. Are there any relationships or habits in your life where you have been unjust or wasteful, yet have justified it to yourself? Will you commit to examining them honestly and seeking to rectify them before you meet your Lord?

The Necessity of Divine Law for Property Regulation
Imam al-Nasafi proves the need for divine law from property disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine law (shar') is necessary to specify property rights and prevent societal collapse.

Core Idea
Without divine law, property disputes destroy humanity and necessitate foolishness in creation.

Imam al-Nasafi argues that property rights cannot be left to human nature alone. He uses a reductio ad absurdum: without a divinely ordained specification, chaos and extinction would result, making creation pointless. This leads to the necessary conclusion that divine law must exist to establish order and prevent attributing foolishness to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall argument. Notice the two parts: the hypothetical disaster and the conclusion requiring divine law.
The passage uses a conditional 'were it not for' to set up the problem.
2
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
Identify the chain of consequences: no specification → greedy hands extend → disputes → enmity → fighting → extinction → creation for extinction → foolishness. Assess why this is impossible for Allah.
3
Understand the solution: divine law
See how Imam al-Nasafi concludes that a divine law must clarify specification and prohibit disputes, preserving order and system in the world.
He mentions 'shar'' (law) as the necessary remedy.
4
Reflect on the consequences of denying shar'
The author explicitly condemns those who reject divine law as promoting chaos and attributing foolishness to Allah. Consider the theological stakes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلولا الشيء الموجب اختصاص من(١) اختص بسبب الملك لها لامتدت الأيدي الخاطفة الخاطئة والأطماع الفاسدة إلى كل من ذلك ولوقعت بينهم المنازعة المولدة(٢) للعداوة والبغضاء المورثة للضغائن والأحقاد، وكل ذلك مما يحمل على القتال وهو يؤدي إلى ما بيننا من انقطاع النسل وارتفاع الجنس المقصود بتخليق العالم، وذلك يوجب أن خلق الخلق لم يكن إلا للفناء خاصة وهو سفه جل ربنا عن ذلك وتعالى. (٣) التملك فإذا لا بد من شرع يبين اختصاص كل من البشر بما له من أصناف النعم بسبب(٣) يمتاز به عن غيره، ويحظر على من سواه ممن لا حظ له من سبب لتلك الأشياء منازعة أربابها المختصين بها لاختصاصهم بأسباب توجب لهم الملك فيها ليندفع الجدال ويرتفع الخصام، ويبقى العالم على ما له من الترتيب والنظام. فمن أنكر الشرع وأبطل الأمر والنهي فقد سعى في إثارة العبث والفساد في العالم، وإثبات أسباب التقاتل والتفاني، ونسب صانع العالم إلى السفه والجهل تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا.
Were it not for the thing that necessitates the specification of whoever is specified by a cause for ownership, then the rapacious seizing hands and corrupt desires would extend to all of that, and disputes would occur among them generating enmity and hatred, bequeathing rancor and grudges, and all of that leads to fighting, which leads to what is between us of the cutting off of offspring and the removal of the species intended by the creation of the world. That necessitates that the creation of creatures was only for annihilation specifically, and that is foolishness—our Lord is exalted above that and transcendent. (3) Ownership: So there must be a law (shar') that clarifies the specification of each human being to the types of blessings he has, by a cause by which he is distinguished from others, and it prohibits those who have no share in a cause for those things from disputing with their owners who are specified by them because of their specification by causes that necessitate ownership for them, so that argumentation is repelled, contention is lifted, and the world remains in its order and system. Whoever denies the law (shar') and nullifies command and prohibition has sought to stir up frivolity and corruption in the world, and to establish causes of fighting and mutual destruction, and has attributed to the Maker of the world foolishness and ignorance—exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a proof from Imam al-Nasafi for the necessity of divine law (shar') based on the problem of property rights. He begins by stating that without a factor specifying who owns what, people would seize everything by force, leading to disputes, enmity, and ultimately warfare. This would result in the extinction of the human race, which is the purpose of creation, making creation pointless—a notion he declares as foolishness (safah) and exalts Allah above it. Then, under the heading 'al-tamalluk' (ownership), he concludes that a divine law is indispensable to specify each person's entitlement to their blessings through a distinguishing cause, prohibiting others from contesting them. This law ends conflict and preserves worldly order. He ends by condemning anyone who rejects divine law as promoting chaos and attributing foolishness to Allah.

Key Terms
شرع (shar')
ملك (ownership)
اختصاص (specification)
عداوة (enmity)
سفه (foolishness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Property rights can be adequately established solely by human convention or natural ethics without divine guidance.
Correction: According to Imam al-Nasafi, without a divine specifying cause, human nature leads to conflict and extinction, proving that divine law is indispensable for order.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen if there were no cause specifying ownership?
A) People would share property equally.
B) Conflict and fighting would lead to the extinction of the human race.
C) Everyone would voluntarily respect each other's property.
D) The world would still be orderly without law.

Key Takeaway
Divine law is necessary to specify property rights; without it, human conflict leads to extinction, which would attribute foolishness to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel entitled to something that is not rightfully yours, do you realize you are acting as if there is no divine law? How does this passage expose the hidden greed in your heart?
?
If you were left to your own reasoning about property, would you be one of the 'greedy hands' that seizes what it wants? What does that tell you about your need for Allah's guidance in every aspect of life?
?
Rejecting divine law, even in a small matter, is to imply that Allah created this world for futility. Where in your life are you subtly rejecting His commands, and how will you repent tonight?

The Irrationality of Neglecting Reason and the Need for Prophets
Al-Nasafi argues from human psychology to the necessity of prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why no rational person would remain in ignorance and how this points to the need for prophets.

Core Idea
Reason demands virtue, but ignorance and desire force us to seek a guide.

Before arguing from reason's limitations, Al-Nasafi paints a vivid picture: no rational person would willingly stay ignorant and indulgent when knowledge and honor are within reach. But human nature pulls toward base desires, so we need external guidance — a prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how Al-Nasafi appeals to common sense: no rational person would be pleased with ignorance and sin.
2
Identify the conflict
The passage contrasts the ideal rational pursuit of virtue with the pull of the commanding soul toward lust and ignorance. See how this tension creates the need for a mediator.
3
Understand the conclusion
Al-Nasafi concludes that this situation necessitates belief in messengers. Ask yourself why a prophet is the solution to this inner conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه لا عاقل في الشاهد يرضى من نفسه الانهماك في الشهوات وإعطاء النفس الأمارة بالسوء جميع ما تتمنى من الفواحش وتهوى من القبائح والاشتغال بالعبث والفساد، وظلم من سواه من العباد وإهمال العقل الذي هو من واهب الله الجزيلة ومنائحة الجليلة عن التفكر في حقائق الأشياء، واكتساب العلوم التي بإحرازها واقتنائها تشرف النفوس وتعظم الأخطار(٤) ويجل في أعين الخلق، ويترأس على دهماء الناس، وتصرف الوجوه إليها بالتعظيم والإجلال وتوجب صدورهم عن آرائهم فيما يحزبهم من الأمور والاقتضاء بهم فيما يأتون به ويذرونه، أو يرضى بنقيصة الجهل مع التمكن من دفعها والنزول على الدون من الدرجات معها في الوسع من الترقي إلى أعلاها، بل كل ذلك من العقلاء في عقولهم الاجتهاد على الإقبال على ما هو يحمد عليه من الأفعال، ويورث له الحمد والثناء الجميل ولسان الصدق والإعراض عما يوجب له ضده هذه الأحوال مما جبل كل منهم على الجهل الذي يعطيه بما يرجو به نجاته ونصره فيما به يطمع نفسه(١). ولا شك أن ما طبع عليه عقله مع ما جبل عليه هو من الجهل يخرجانه(٢) إلى من يعلم عواقب الأمور حتى يروض نفسه ويحملها على ما يدعو إليه عقله وينزجر عما يزجره (عنه عقله)(٣) على إشاراته دون أن يهملها(٤) وما هي بجبلتها تهواه وتميل إليه، وذلك يوجب القول بالرسل عليهم السلام لا محالة. يحقق هذا أن العقل في طبعه الميل إلى المحاسن والنفرة عن القبائح، ثم هو يقف على جمل المحاسن والمساوئ دون أعيانها ، وكلياتها دون إفرادها ثم بعد هذا نقرر الكلام من وجهين :أحدهما أن الشرف والحكمة من الوقوف على أعيان ذلك دون الجهل إذ بذلك يمتاز الحكيم الماهر عن الغبي الجاهل(٩) فلا بد من ورود البيان مما له العلم بحقيقة كل فرد من أفراد تلك الجمل أنه من جملة المحاسن أو من جملة القبائح ليحمل(٦) العقل بميلانه إلى المحاسن صاحبه على مباشرته وتنافره عن القبائح على الانتهاء
And after that, there is no rational person in the witnessed world who would be pleased with himself for indulging in lusts, giving the commanding soul all that it desires of obscenities and inclines to of ugly acts, occupying himself with frivolity and corruption, oppressing others among the servants, and neglecting the intellect which is from the great gifts of Allah and the noble endowment, from reflecting on the realities of things, and acquiring sciences through which, by their attainment and possession, souls become noble, and dangers become great, and one becomes esteemed in the eyes of creation, and becomes a leader over the masses, and faces turn toward him with veneration and reverence, and they oblige their opinions to his regarding matters that befall them, and they follow him in what they do and leave. Or he would be pleased with the deficiency of ignorance while having the ability to repel it, and descending to the lowliest ranks while it is within his capacity to ascend to the highest. Rather, all of that is from the rational ones (intellects) in their minds—striving to engage in actions for which he is praised, which bequeath for him praise, beautiful commendation, and a tongue of truth, and turning away from what necessitates the opposite of these states, from what each of them is disposed by nature—from the ignorance that gives him what he hopes for his salvation and victory in that which his soul covets. There is no doubt that what his intellect is naturally disposed to, along with what he is disposed to from ignorance, both bring him out to one who knows the consequences of matters, so that he disciplines himself and forces himself to what his intellect calls him to, and refrains from what his intellect rebukes him from, by his signals, without neglecting it, and what it (the soul) is by its nature inclined to and desires. That necessitates belief in the messengers (peace be upon them) without doubt. This is verified by the fact that the intellect, by its nature, has an inclination towards good things (maḥāsin) and aversion from evil things (qabā’iḥ). Then it stops at the generalities (jūmal) of goods and evils, not their particulars (a‘yān), and at their universals (kulliyāt), not their individual instances (āfād). Then after this, we present the discourse from two aspects: one of them is that nobility and wisdom consist in knowing the particulars of that, not ignorance, because by that the skilled wise one is distinguished from the stupid ignorant one. So there must be an exposition from one who has knowledge of the reality of every individual of those generalities, whether it is from the category of good or from the category of evil, so that the intellect, by its inclination towards goods, drives its possessor to practice it, and by its aversion from evils, [drives him] to desist.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of prophethood. Al-Nasafi first argues that no rational person would willingly remain in ignorance and base desires when he can attain knowledge, honor, and leadership. Reason pushes him towards virtuous actions, but due to his natural inclination to ignorance and desire, he needs someone who knows the outcomes of things—a prophet—to guide him. Then he verifies this: reason by nature loves good and hates evil, but it only knows general categories, not specific instances. A prophet is needed to specify which particular actions are good or evil. He then presents the first of two arguments: wisdom and nobility consist in knowing particular goods and evils, so divine revelation is necessary to provide that detailed knowledge.

Key Terms
عقل
رسل
نفس أمارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that reason alone can fully overcome human desires and lead to salvation without external guidance.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that while reason points to virtue, the innate ignorance and desires force us to seek a prophet who knows the consequences and can guide us.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Nasafi say a person must go to 'one who knows the consequences of matters'?
A) Because reason is completely unreliable.
B) Because innate ignorance and desire need an external disciplinarian.
C) Because prophets are the only source of moral knowledge.
D) Because the intellect cannot know any good or evil.

Key Takeaway
The internal conflict between reason and desire necessitates a prophetic guide who knows the outcomes of actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Al-Nasafi says no rational person would be pleased with indulging lusts while neglecting intellect. Yet many do. What does this say about your own state? Are you truly following your intellect or rationalizing your desires?

Reason's General Knowledge and the Necessity of Specific Revelation
Al-Nasafi verifies that reason knows only general good/evil, necessitating prophets for specific guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how reason's limitation to general goods and evils proves the need for prophets to specify particular actions.

Core Idea
Reason knows only general moral categories; only revelation can specify which actions are good or evil.

Al-Nasafi now makes his core epistemological argument: the intellect by nature loves good and hates evil, but it only knows these in a general way. Good and evil in their particular instances require specification from a source that knows every detail—a prophet. This lesson unpacks that limitation and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the verification passage
Read the Arabic and translation from 'يحقق هذا' to the end. Notice how Al-Nasafi confirms the previous point by analyzing reason's scope.
2
Identify the key distinction
The text distinguishes between generalities (جمل) and particulars (أعيان), universals (كليات) and individuals (أفراد). Reason knows the first set but not the second.
3
Understand the first proof
The author presents the first of two proofs: nobility consists in knowing particular goods/evils, not ignorance. So we need someone who knows every individual instance to inform us.
4
Connect to prophethood
Ask yourself: why does this argument lead to the necessity of prophets? Because only a divine messenger can have that detailed knowledge of what is truly good or evil in every situation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه لا عاقل في الشاهد يرضى من نفسه الانهماك في الشهوات وإعطاء النفس الأمارة بالسوء جميع ما تتمنى من الفواحش وتهوى من القبائح والاشتغال بالعبث والفساد، وظلم من سواه من العباد وإهمال العقل الذي هو من واهب الله الجزيلة ومنائحة الجليلة عن التفكر في حقائق الأشياء، واكتساب العلوم التي بإحرازها واقتنائها تشرف النفوس وتعظم الأخطار(٤) ويجل في أعين الخلق، ويترأس على دهماء الناس، وتصرف الوجوه إليها بالتعظيم والإجلال وتوجب صدورهم عن آرائهم فيما يحزبهم من الأمور والاقتضاء بهم فيما يأتون به ويذرونه، أو يرضى بنقيصة الجهل مع التمكن من دفعها والنزول على الدون من الدرجات معها في الوسع من الترقي إلى أعلاها، بل كل ذلك من العقلاء في عقولهم الاجتهاد على الإقبال على ما هو يحمد عليه من الأفعال، ويورث له الحمد والثناء الجميل ولسان الصدق والإعراض عما يوجب له ضده هذه الأحوال مما جبل كل منهم على الجهل الذي يعطيه بما يرجو به نجاته ونصره فيما به يطمع نفسه(١). ولا شك أن ما طبع عليه عقله مع ما جبل عليه هو من الجهل يخرجانه(٢) إلى من يعلم عواقب الأمور حتى يروض نفسه ويحملها على ما يدعو إليه عقله وينزجر عما يزجره (عنه عقله)(٣) على إشاراته دون أن يهملها(٤) وما هي بجبلتها تهواه وتميل إليه، وذلك يوجب القول بالرسل عليهم السلام لا محالة. يحقق هذا أن العقل في طبعه الميل إلى المحاسن والنفرة عن القبائح، ثم هو يقف على جمل المحاسن والمساوئ دون أعيانها ، وكلياتها دون إفرادها ثم بعد هذا نقرر الكلام من وجهين :أحدهما أن الشرف والحكمة من الوقوف على أعيان ذلك دون الجهل إذ بذلك يمتاز الحكيم الماهر عن الغبي الجاهل(٩) فلا بد من ورود البيان مما له العلم بحقيقة كل فرد من أفراد تلك الجمل أنه من جملة المحاسن أو من جملة القبائح ليحمل(٦) العقل بميلانه إلى المحاسن صاحبه على مباشرته وتنافره عن القبائح على الانتهاء
And after that, there is no rational person in the witnessed world who would be pleased with himself for indulging in lusts, giving the commanding soul all that it desires of obscenities and inclines to of ugly acts, occupying himself with frivolity and corruption, oppressing others among the servants, and neglecting the intellect which is from the great gifts of Allah and the noble endowment, from reflecting on the realities of things, and acquiring sciences through which, by their attainment and possession, souls become noble, and dangers become great, and one becomes esteemed in the eyes of creation, and becomes a leader over the masses, and faces turn toward him with veneration and reverence, and they oblige their opinions to his regarding matters that befall them, and they follow him in what they do and leave. Or he would be pleased with the deficiency of ignorance while having the ability to repel it, and descending to the lowliest ranks while it is within his capacity to ascend to the highest. Rather, all of that is from the rational ones (intellects) in their minds—striving to engage in actions for which he is praised, which bequeath for him praise, beautiful commendation, and a tongue of truth, and turning away from what necessitates the opposite of these states, from what each of them is disposed by nature—from the ignorance that gives him what he hopes for his salvation and victory in that which his soul covets. There is no doubt that what his intellect is naturally disposed to, along with what he is disposed to from ignorance, both bring him out to one who knows the consequences of matters, so that he disciplines himself and forces himself to what his intellect calls him to, and refrains from what his intellect rebukes him from, by his signals, without neglecting it, and what it (the soul) is by its nature inclined to and desires. That necessitates belief in the messengers (peace be upon them) without doubt. This is verified by the fact that the intellect, by its nature, has an inclination towards good things (maḥāsin) and aversion from evil things (qabā’iḥ). Then it stops at the generalities (jūmal) of goods and evils, not their particulars (a‘yān), and at their universals (kulliyāt), not their individual instances (āfād). Then after this, we present the discourse from two aspects: one of them is that nobility and wisdom consist in knowing the particulars of that, not ignorance, because by that the skilled wise one is distinguished from the stupid ignorant one. So there must be an exposition from one who has knowledge of the reality of every individual of those generalities, whether it is from the category of good or from the category of evil, so that the intellect, by its inclination towards goods, drives its possessor to practice it, and by its aversion from evils, [drives him] to desist.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of prophethood. Al-Nasafi first argues that no rational person would willingly remain in ignorance and base desires when he can attain knowledge, honor, and leadership. Reason pushes him towards virtuous actions, but due to his natural inclination to ignorance and desire, he needs someone who knows the outcomes of things—a prophet—to guide him. Then he verifies this: reason by nature loves good and hates evil, but it only knows general categories, not specific instances. A prophet is needed to specify which particular actions are good or evil. He then presents the first of two arguments: wisdom and nobility consist in knowing particular goods and evils, so divine revelation is necessary to provide that detailed knowledge.

Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that reason only knows general categories; specific judgments require revelation from a prophet.

Key Takeaway
Reason knows the categories of good and evil, but only revelation can specify which particular actions belong to each category.

Rational Ambition and the Need for Prophets
Nasafi argues from rational ambition to the necessity of prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses rational psychology to argue that human beings inevitably need prophets to guide them.

Core Idea
Rational ambition alone cannot overcome ignorance; we need prophets who know consequences.

This passage from Imam Nasafi presents a unique argument for prophethood based on human nature. He observes that rational people naturally seek honor and avoid disgrace, yet they are also prone to ignorance and base desires. This tension, he argues, makes prophets essential as guides who know the true outcomes of actions. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own description of rational ambition and innate ignorance come through.
Notice the contrast between seeking praise and being disposed to ignorance.
2
Identify the two forces
Nasafi describes two opposing forces: the rational pursuit of praiseworthy actions and the innate inclination to ignorance and desires. Identify where each is mentioned.
3
Understand the need for a guide
The passage states that these forces drive the person to seek 'one who knows the consequences of things' (من يعلم عواقب الأمور). Ask yourself: why is knowledge of consequences necessary?
This refers to a prophet who can reveal unseen outcomes.
4
Reflect on the necessity
Nasafi concludes that this 'necessarily requires affirming the messengers' (يوجب القول بالرسل لا محالة). Consider what 'necessarily' implies about the strength of the argument.
The necessity is rational, not just religious.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويترأس على دهماء الناس، وتصرف الوجوه إليها بالتعظيم والإجلال وتوجب صدورهم عن آرائهم فيما يحزبهم من الأمور والاقتضاء بهم فيما يأتون به ويذرونه، أو يرضى بنقيصة الجهل مع التمكن من دفعها والنزول على الدون من الدرجات معها في الوسع من الترقي إلى أعلاها، بل كل ذلك من العقلاء في عقولهم الاجتهاد على الإقبال على ما هو يحمد عليه من الأفعال، ويورث له الحمد والثناء الجميل ولسان الصدق والإعراض عما يوجب له ضده هذه الأحوال مما جبل كل منهم على الجهل الذي يعطيه بما يرجو به نجاته ونصره فيما به يطمع نفسه. ولا شك أن ما طبع عليه عقله مع ما جبل عليه هو من الجهل يخرجانه إلى من يعلم عواقب الأمور حتى يروض نفسه ويحملها على ما يدعو إليه عقله وينزجر عما يزجره عنه عقله على إشاراته دون أن يهملها وما هي بجبلتها تهواه وتميل إليه، وذلك يوجب القول بالرسل عليهم السلام لا محالة.
And he assumes leadership over the common people, and faces are turned towards him with veneration and exaltation, and he causes them to defer to his opinions in matters that befall them and to follow him in what they do and leave, or he is content with the defect of ignorance despite being able to remove it, and descends to a lower rank when he could ascend to a higher one. Rather, all rational people, by their reason, strive to pursue actions that are praiseworthy, which bring them praise, good repute, and a truthful tongue, and to turn away from what brings them the opposite of these states, [given] what each of them has been disposed to of ignorance that leads him to hope for deliverance and victory in what his soul desires. There is no doubt that his nature and innate disposition, due to ignorance, drive him to one who knows the consequences of things, so that he may discipline his soul and compel it to follow what his reason calls him to, and refrain from what his reason forbids him, following its indications without neglecting them, nor what his nature inclines to and desires. And that necessarily requires affirming the messengers, peace be upon them.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for the necessity of prophets based on rational psychology. The author, Nasafi, observes that rational people naturally seek honor and leadership, and they would not willingly accept ignorance when they have the ability to overcome it. Instead, they strive for praiseworthy actions. However, human nature is also disposed to ignorance and base desires. Therefore, in order to discipline the soul and follow reason's guidance, people need someone who knows the true consequences of actions—a prophet. This necessity is presented as inevitable (لا محالة). The argument proceeds from a description of rational ambition to the conclusion that prophets are required to guide humanity.

Reason Knows Only General Good and Evil
Nasafi argues that reason's limitation necessitates prophetic specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reason alone cannot determine the specific moral value of actions and why prophethood is necessary for divine guidance.

Core Idea
Reason knows the big picture, not the details—prophets fill the gap.

In this passage, Nasafi presents a key argument for the necessity of prophethood. He begins by affirming reason’s natural inclination towards good and aversion to evil, but then points out a significant limitation: reason only grasps the general categories, not the specific instances. This sets the stage for his first proof that divine revelation is needed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Nasafi first states the ability of reason, then immediately limits it.
Pay attention to the contrast between 'جمل' (generals) and 'أعيان' (specifics).
2
Identify the problem
Nasafi says reason knows 'جمل' (generals) but not 'أعيان' (specifics). Why is that a problem? Consider what honor and wisdom require.
The passage connects honor and wisdom to knowing specific instances.
3
Understand the solution
The solution is revelation (بيان) from one who knows every individual action. This shows the necessity of prophethood.
Reflect on why this makes prophethood essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحقق هذا أن العقل في طبعه الميل إلى المحاسن والنفرة عن القبائح، ثم هو يقف على جمل المحاسن والمساوئ دون أعيانها، وكلياتها دون إفرادها ثم بعد هذا نقرر الكلام من وجهين: أحدهما أن الشرف والحكمة من الوقوف على أعيان ذلك دون الجهل إذ بذلك يمتاز الحكيم الماهر عن الغبي الجاهل فلا بد من ورود البيان مما له العلم بحقيقة كل فرد من أفراد تلك الجمل أنه من جملة المحاسن أو من جملة القبائح ليحمل العقل بميلانه إلى المحاسن صاحبه على مباشرته وتنافره عن القبائح على الانتهاء عنه لولا ذلك لم يحصل لتخليق العقلاء مائلاً إلى المحاسن نافرا عن القبائح عاقبة.
This is confirmed by the fact that reason, by its nature, inclines towards good deeds and recoils from evil deeds, yet it only grasps the general categories of good and evil, not their specific instances; their universals, not their particulars. Then after this we establish the discussion from two aspects: One of them is that honor and wisdom consist in knowing the specific instances of these things rather than ignorance, for thereby the wise and skilled person is distinguished from the stupid and ignorant. Thus, there must come a statement from one who has knowledge of the true nature of every individual of those categories—whether it belongs to the category of good or to the category of evil—so that reason, by its inclination towards good, may cause its possessor to perform it, and by its revulsion from evil, may cause him to refrain from it. Otherwise, the creation of rational beings as inclined towards good and averse to evil would have no beneficial result.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is the first of two arguments (وجهان) that confirm the need for prophets. Nasafi states that reason naturally loves good and hates evil, but it only knows these in general terms (جمل, كليات), not in their specific instances (أعيان, أفراد). The first argument is based on honor and wisdom: a truly wise person knows the specific good and evil of particular actions. Since reason cannot do this on its own, there must be a divine statement (بيان) from someone who knows the reality of each action—a prophet—to specify which actions fall under good or evil. Without this specification, the innate inclination of rational beings towards good would be futile.

المحاسن
القبائح
الجمل
الأعيان
البيان

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That reason can fully determine the moral value of every action.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that reason only knows moral categories in general, not the specific moral status of individual actions.

Key Takeaway
Reason knows moral categories in general, but only divine revelation can specify which particular actions are good or evil.

Why Reason Needs Prophets: Nasafi's Argument from General Command
Nasafi argues that rational command needs prophetic specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that reason's general command and prohibition require prophetic specification to be actionable, and why this demonstrates the necessity of prophets.

Core Idea
Reason's general command needs prophetic details to be effective.

In this passage, Nasafi presents the second rational argument for the necessity of prophethood. He compares the general command of reason to a general revelation that needs explanation. Without a prophet to specify which actions are good or evil, the rational command would remain vague and ineffective, contradicting divine wisdom. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand reason's inclination
Start by noting that reason naturally inclines to good and recoils from evil, but cannot identify specific good and evil acts in their true nature. This sets up the problem.
This is the basis of the argument.
2
Define command and prohibition
Nasafi defines command and prohibition as a call to perform or abstain. Reason inherently issues such a call through its inclination.
3
See reason as proof of God's command
God created reason with these inclinations, so reason serves as a proof (dalil) of God's command and prohibition, similar to revealed speech.
An important analogy: reason like revelation.
4
Identify the specification gap
Since reason cannot know the specific status of each individual action, it does not know if a particular act is commanded or prohibited. Thus its command is general (mujmal).
The crucial problem.
5
Conclude the need for a prophet
Without a prophetic explanation (bayan sam'i), the rational command would be ineffective, like a general revelation without explanation. A messenger is necessary for wisdom to be fulfilled.
This completes the argument for prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكذا العاقل لو ترك ومجرد عقله مع أنه يميل إلى المحاسن وينفر عن القبائح ولا وقوف له على أفرادها بحقائقها من الحسن والقبح، وهو بميلانه إلى الحسن يدعو صاحبه إلى اكتسابه، وبنفاره ينهي صاحبه عن الإقدام عليه، وذلك هو معنى الأمر والنهي، إذ الأمر والنهي ليس إلا الدعاء إلى المباشرة والامتناع، والله تعالى هو الذي خلقه مائلاً إلى ما يميل إليه، نافرًا عما ينفر عنه، فيكون العقل دليل أمر الله ونهيه كالخطاب دليل أمره الذي هو صفته القائمة بذاته لما فيه من الدعاء إلى مباشرة فعل ودليل نهيه لما فيه من الدعاء إلى الامتناع عن مباشرة فعل العقل، ثم هو لما لم يقف على حقيقة كل فرد من أفراد العقل أنه من قبيل المحاسن أو من قبيل القبائح لم يعلم أنه مأمور به أو منهي عنه، فلو لم يرد الشرع ببيان محل كل فرد من أفراد الأفعال أنه من قبيل المحاسن أو من قبيل القبائح لبقى الأمر والنهي الحاصلان بالعقل على وجه لا يطاوع العمل بهما بمنزلة الأمر والنهي الواردين بالخطاب على سبيل الإجمال ولو لم يتصل البيان بالمجمل لما اهتدى المكلف إلى رفة ما أمر به ونهى عنه، ولا يتوصل إلى الائتمار والانتهاء، وذلك خارج عن حد الحكمة، فلابد حينئذ من ورود بيان سمعي على لسان من ارتضاه الصانع سفيراً بينه وبين عباده لتتحقق الحكمة في ذلك.
Thus, if the rational person were left solely to his mere reason, [we would note that] while he inclines towards good and recoils from evil, he has no knowledge of the specific instances of these in their true nature of goodness and badness. And by his inclination towards good, he calls his possessor to acquire it, and by his revulsion he forbids his possessor from embarking upon it. This is the meaning of command and prohibition, for command and prohibition are nothing but a call to perform or to abstain. And God Almighty is the one who created him inclining towards what he inclines to and recoiling from what he recoils from. Thus, reason becomes a proof of God's command and prohibition, just as the revealed speech (al-khitab) is a proof of His command, which is His attribute subsisting in His essence, because it contains a call to perform an action, and a proof of His prohibition because it contains a call to abstain from performing an action of reason. Then, since reason does not grasp the true nature of every individual action—whether it belongs to the category of good or evil—it does not know whether it is commanded or prohibited. So if the divine law (al-shar') did not come with an explanation of the status of every individual action—that it belongs to the category of good or evil—then the command and prohibition obtained through reason would remain in such a way that action would not comply with them, analogous to command and prohibition coming through revelation in a general manner (ijmal). And if the explanation were not connected to the general, the responsible person (mukallaf) would not be guided to the details of what he is commanded and forbidden, nor would he be able to obey or abstain. That is outside the bounds of wisdom. Therefore, there must necessarily come a revealed statement (bayan sam'i) upon the tongue of one whom the Maker has chosen as an envoy between Himself and His servants, so that wisdom may be realized in that.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is the second of the two arguments (الوجه الثاني) supporting the need for prophets. Nasafi draws an analogy: reason, by its nature, issues a general command to do good and a general prohibition against evil, but it cannot specify which particular actions are good or evil. God created reason as a proof (دليل) of His command and prohibition, similar to how revealed speech (الخطاب) is a proof of His command. However, just as a general revelation without detailed explanation would leave the responsible person unable to obey properly, so also the general command of reason needs specification. This specification must come through a revealed statement (بيان سمعي) on the tongue of a chosen messenger (سفير), ensuring that the command is actionable and wisdom is fulfilled. The argument emphasizes that without prophets, the rational command would be ineffective and divine wisdom would be compromised.

Key Terms
الأمر والنهي
العقل
الخطاب
الإجمال
البيان السمعي
السفير
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That reason's general command is sufficient for moral obligation and guidance.
Correction: Nasafi shows that without prophetic specification, the rational command remains ineffective and cannot lead to action, thus reason alone is insufficient.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what would happen if there were no prophetic explanation for rational command and prohibition?
A) Reason would still be fully effective.
B) The command and prohibition would remain general and not lead to action, which is against wisdom.
C) People would invent their own explanations.
D) God would not hold anyone accountable.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi argues that without prophetic specification, reason's command remains general and ineffective, thus proving that prophets are necessary for divine wisdom to be fulfilled.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The lesson says that reason's general command is ineffective without prophetic details. Is there a command of Allah that you know in general but have not implemented because you lack the specific details? What step will you take today to learn that detail so you can obey fully?
?
If you believe that divine wisdom requires prophets to specify the moral law, how does that affect your attitude towards the sunnah of the Prophet (peace be upon him)? Do you treat his teachings as necessary specifications or as optional advice? Give a concrete example from your daily life.

Gratitude to the Benefactor and the Necessity of Prophets
Nasafi argues that rational obligation of gratitude necessitates prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rational obligation of gratitude to God logically entails the need for prophetic guidance.

Core Idea
Reason knows gratitude is obligatory; wisdom requires prophets to show how.

Having established that reason can know moral obligations, Nasafi now argues that the very fact that gratitude to God is rationally obligatory means that God must send messengers. If gratitude were left unguided, divine wisdom would be undermined. This short but dense passage bridges rational ethics and prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the literal translation. Notice the strong declarations: 'good and obligatory', 'evil and forbidden'.
2
Understand the rational claim
Nasafi asserts that reason itself judges gratitude as obligatory. This is a key Maturidi position. Identify what makes ingratitude evil.
3
Connect to divine wisdom
The second sentence uses divine wisdom (hikma) to argue that God cannot leave gratitude unfulfilled. Why would that require prophets?
4
Reflect on the need for prophets
Consider: reason alone may know gratitude is owed, but how to express it? Prophets specify the acts of worship that constitute proper gratitude.
This is the implicit conclusion of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يحقق هذا أن شكر المنعم حسن واجب في العقول وكفران المنعم قبيح محرم فيها، لا يليق بحكمة الحكيم إسقاط الشكر وإباحة الكفران.
And what confirms this is that gratitude to the Benefactor is good and obligatory in the intellects, and ingratitude to the Benefactor is evil and forbidden in them. It is not befitting the wisdom of the Wise to abandon gratitude and permit ingratitude.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a concise argument for the necessity of prophets based on the rational obligation of gratitude (شكر المنعم). Nasafi asserts that reason independently knows that thanking the Benefactor (God) is good and obligatory, and that ingratitude is evil and forbidden. Given this rational knowledge, divine wisdom cannot allow gratitude to be neglected or ingratitude to be permitted. Therefore, to fulfill this obligation, God must send messengers to guide people in how to properly express gratitude, since reason alone may not know the specific modes of gratitude (e.g., acts of worship). This argument ties back to the earlier discussion: prophets are needed to specify the actions that constitute gratitude.

Key Terms

الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: Nasafi argues that while reason knows the obligation, it does not know the specific acts of gratitude; divine wisdom requires that God provide guidance on how to properly thank Him, which is done through prophets.

Checkpoint

A) It is permissible.
B) It is evil and forbidden.
C) It is a private matter.
D) It is sometimes good.

Key Takeaway
Reason knows gratitude is obligatory; divine wisdom requires prophets to show its proper forms.

Human Need for Prophets Despite Reason
Imam Nasafi's proof of prophet necessity from human limitation and universal consultation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi argues from human deficiency and universal consultation to prove the necessity of prophets.

Core Idea
Even the brightest mind needs a guide – that is why prophets are sent.

Imam al-Nasafi addresses a key question: If humans have reason, why do they need prophets? He argues that no matter how intelligent a person is, human limitations (al-naqṣ al-basharī) mean they cannot know everything on their own. The universal practice of people seeking advice and learning from each other shows that even the best minds rely on others. This points to the greater need for divine messengers who provide guidance for both worldly and religious affairs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a claim, evidence from common practice, and a conclusion.
2
Understand the premise
Imam al-Nasafi states that even the most intelligent person needs a guide due to human deficiency. Reflect on why this is a crucial starting point.
3
Examine the evidence
The passage cites the universal custom of consultation among rational people. Why does this serve as proof? Think about how it shows inherent limitation.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The evidence leads to the conclusion that prophets are necessary. Note how the argument connects the universal human need to the role of messengers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحققه أن أوفر الخليقة عقلاً وأذكاهم فهما وأصفاهم قريحة يحتاج في أكثر أمور دينه ودنياه إلى هاد يهديه، ومرشد يرشده لاستيلاء النقص البشري على جبلته، وقصور عقله عن الوقوف على ما به إليه حاجة من مصالحه، ولهذا جرى العرف بين العقلاء من لدن بدء الخليقة إلى زماننا هذا، باستشارة بعضهم بعضا و اختلاف البعض إلى البعض، ليزداد فيما يحتاج إليه بإشارة غيره من ذوي البصائر إليه بصيرة بل ليستفيد بذلك منه علمًا ، لا ينكر هذا إلا معاند مكابر. وذلك دليل الحاجة إلى الرسل عليهم السلام، وأن لا غنية بالعقلاء عنهم لمصالح داريهم.
He confirms it: that the most abundant of creation in intellect, the sharpest in understanding, and the purest in natural disposition need, in most of the affairs of their religion and their worldly life, a guide to guide them and a mentor to direct them, due to the dominance of human deficiency over their nature and the incapacity of their intellect to grasp what they need of their interests. And for this reason, the custom has prevailed among rational people from the beginning of creation until this time of consulting one another and visiting one another, so that one may increase in insight regarding what he needs by the indication of others who possess insight, rather he may thereby attain knowledge from him. None denies this except a stubborn obdurate person. And that is evidence of the need for the messengers (peace be upon them), and that rational people have no self-sufficiency from them for the interests of their two abodes.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy and common practice. Imam al-Nasafi argues that no matter how intelligent a person is, human deficiency (al-naqṣ al-basharī) limits them, and they need guidance from others in most religious and worldly matters. The universal practice of seeking advice and learning from others—even the best minds—demonstrates this need. From this, he concludes that humans need messengers (rusul) from Allah, who are the ultimate guides, and that reason alone cannot provide for all interests in this life and the next.

Key Terms

هاد
مرشد
استشارة
الرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person with a strong intellect does not need external guidance.
Correction: The passage shows that even the brightest individuals need guidance from others due to inherent human deficiency, which is why universal consultation exists.

Checkpoint

B) The universal human practice of consulting and learning from one another, showing inherent limitation.
C) The fact that some people are foolish.
D) The claim that reason is completely useless.

Key Takeaway
Human beings, no matter how intelligent, are inherently limited and require guidance from others; this universal principle demonstrates the necessity of divine messengers.

Reason Cannot Equal Divine Knowledge
Nasafi proves that reason cannot attain all beneficial knowledge, necessitating revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human reason is insufficient for complete guidance and why revelation is necessary.

Core Idea
Reason's limitation proves the necessity of revelation.

Imam al-Nasafi presents a simple yet powerful argument: people differ in knowledge, so it is unreasonable to claim that reason alone suffices. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for the necessity of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Let the argument stand as the author intended before analyzing it.
Notice the confident opening phrase 'يحققه أن' indicating a conclusive proof.
2
Understand the premise: rational people differ
Identify the observation that rational people (العقلاء) vary in their knowledge of what is beneficial. This is an undeniable fact.
This variation is the foundation of the proof.
3
Follow the logical conclusion
If humans differ, it is possible that Allah possesses knowledge of benefits that no human has, which He conveys through messengers. This establishes the need for revelation.
The argument moves from observed human limitation to divine provision.
4
Consider the absurd claim
Anyone who claims self-sufficiency by reason (الغنية بالعقل) is effectively claiming equality with Allah in knowledge—an extreme ignorance.
This reductio ad absurdum shows the seriousness of rejecting revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحققه أن من المعلوم الذي لا ريب فيه أن العقلاء يتفاضلون في إدراك ما به مصالحهم في أمر الدين والدنيا . ويكون عند واحد من ذلك ما ليس عند غيره. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فلا يدفع أن يكون عند الله تعالى شيء مما به صلاح عباده مما ليس عند خلقه ليوصل ذلك إليهم برسله فمن ادعى الغنية بالعقل عن الله تعالى فقد ادعى أنه يساوي الله تعالى في العلم بما به مصالح العباد، ومن هذا دعواه فهو ممن لا غاية في جهله، ولا نهاية لغباوته، والله الهادي.
He confirms it: that it is known without doubt that rational people differ in their perception of what constitutes their interests in matters of religion and worldly life. And one of them has something that another does not. And if the matter is thus, it cannot be denied that Allah Almighty has something by which the well-being of His servants is achieved that His creation does not have, so that He may convey it to them through His messengers. So whoever claims self-sufficiency by intellect from Allah Almighty has claimed that he equals Allah Almighty in knowledge of what constitutes the interests of the servants. And one with such a claim is among those with no limit to his ignorance and no end to his stupidity. And Allah is the Guide.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a proof by gradation and reductio ad absurdum. Imam al-Nasafi notes that rational people (al-ʿuqalāʾ) vary in their knowledge of what is beneficial. Since some have knowledge others lack, it is reasonable that Allah possesses knowledge of benefits that no creature has, which He conveys through prophets. Therefore, to claim that reason alone is sufficient (al-ghinyā bi-al-ʿaql) is to claim equality with Allah in knowledge—an absurd and ignorant position.

Key Terms
تفاضل العقلاء
مصالح
الغنية بالعقل
المساواة بالله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human reason alone is sufficient to discover all moral and religious truths.
Correction: Imam al-Nasafi shows that because rational people differ in knowledge, it cannot be claimed that reason is sufficient; indeed, such a claim implies equality with Allah's knowledge.

Checkpoint
What does Imam al-Nasafi say about someone who claims intellect is sufficient without divine guidance?
A) They are wise.
B) They are claiming equality with Allah in knowledge, which is extreme ignorance.
C) They are following the majority.
D) They are correct in some matters.

Key Takeaway
Human reason is limited and cannot replace divine guidance; to claim otherwise is extreme ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a command in the Qur'an or Sunnah that does not align with your personal reasoning, do you submit to it or do you subconsciously consider it outdated? How does this lesson's warning about 'self-sufficiency by intellect' challenge that tendency?

?
The passage states that claiming self-sufficiency by reason is claiming equality with Allah in knowledge. Has there been an area of life where you have acted as if your understanding is sufficient, ignoring the possibility that Allah might know what is better for you? What step can you take today to actively rely on revelation over your own opinion?

Proof of Need for Prophets from Human Deficiency
Nasafi proves the need for prophets from human deficiency and mutual consultation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that the universal human practice of consultation demonstrates the need for prophets due to inherent human deficiency.

Core Idea
Even the wisest person needs guidance — consultation proves our need for prophets.

Nasafi presents a powerful argument for prophethood grounded in observable human behavior. He begins with a premise about human nature and then points to the universal custom of consultation as evidence. Before reading the passage, consider: why do even intelligent people seek advice? This lesson unpacks Nasafi's reasoning.

Prophets provide guidance beyond human reason.

This practice is undeniable except by a stubborn person. Nasafi concludes that this custom is evidence of the need for prophets, since rational people cannot be independent of them for the interests of both this world and the hereafter.

Inequality of Knowledge and the Need for Prophets
Nasafi proves the need for prophets from inequality of human knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that the observable inequality of human knowledge logically requires the possibility of prophetic revelation.

Core Idea
If humans differ in knowledge, God may have knowledge we don't, conveyed through prophets.

Nasafi presents a second proof for the necessity of prophets, grounded in a simple observation: people differ in what they know about their own well-being. If this is true among humans, it is unreasonable to deny that God possesses knowledge beneficial to us that we lack—and He conveys it through messengers. This lesson walks through that argument and its striking implication: that claiming reason alone suffices is not just arrogance but intellectual absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise: inequality of knowledge
Read the opening sentence where Nasafi states it is known without doubt that rational people differ in perceiving what benefits them in religion and this world. Notice he treats this as a starting point everyone accepts.
The Arabic uses 'تفاضل' which implies a hierarchy of degrees.
2
Follow the inference to God's knowledge
Nasafi then moves from the human case to the divine: if humans differ, it cannot be denied that God possesses something for our well-being that we do not. This is a logical step from observed inequality to the possibility of exclusive divine knowledge.
He uses 'لا يدفع' indicating it is logically undeniable.
3
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
Read the part where Nasafi equates the claim of sufficiency of reason with claiming equality with God in knowledge. Reflect on why this is absurd: our knowledge is limited, God's is infinite.
This is a strong rhetorical move, labeling such a person as utterly ignorant.
4
Connect back to the need for prophets
The whole argument shows that prophets are the necessary channel for God to convey the knowledge we cannot reach by reason alone. Summarize the logical chain in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحققه أن من المعلوم الذي لا ريب فيه أن العقلاء يتفاضلون في إدراك ما به مصالحهم في أمر الدين والدنيا. ويكون عند واحد من ذلك ما ليس عند غيره. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فلا يدفع أن يكون عند الله تعالى شيء مما به صلاح عباده مما ليس عند خلقه ليوصل ذلك إليهم برسله. فمن ادعى الغنية بالعقل عن الله تعالى فقد ادعى أنه يساوي الله تعالى في العلم بما به مصالح العباد، ومن هذا دعواه فهو ممن لا غاية في جهله، ولا نهاية لغباوته، والله الهادي.
He verifies that it is known without doubt that rational people differ in degrees in perceiving what pertains to their interests in matters of religion and the world. And one possesses something of that which another does not. When this is the case, it cannot be denied that God Almighty possesses something of what pertains to the well-being of His servants which His creatures do not possess, so that He may convey that to them through His messengers. Whoever claims independence from God Almighty through reason has claimed that he equals God Almighty in knowledge of what pertains to the interests of servants, and whoever makes this claim is one with no limit to his ignorance and no end to his stupidity, and God is the Guide.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a second proof of the need for prophets, starting from the observable inequality of human knowledge. Nasafi states it is indisputable that rational people differ in their grasp of what is beneficial in religion and worldly affairs—some know what others do not. From this he infers that it cannot be denied that God possesses something beneficial for His servants that is not possessed by His creatures, and He conveys it through prophets. Nasafi then makes a reductio ad absurdum: anyone who claims that reason suffices without God implicitly claims equality with God in knowledge of what benefits people. Such a person is utterly ignorant and foolish. The lesson emphasizes the logical necessity of revelation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: Nasafi argues that this claim implies equality with God's knowledge, which is absurd given the obvious inequality of human knowledge and the limitations of reason.

Checkpoint

A) That reason is perfect and needs no guidance.
B) That humans are individually knowledgeable.
C) That one equals God in knowledge of human interests.
D) That prophets are unnecessary for those with strong intellect.

Key Takeaway
The inequality of human knowledge logically requires the possibility of prophetic revelation, and denying that need amounts to claiming equality with God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Need for Divine Revelation Even for Rational Categories
Nasafi explains the need for revelation even for necessary and impossible acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why even obligatory and impossible acts—which reason can know in principle—require revelation from Allah, and the two specific reasons for this need, particularly the classification of people based on intellectual capacity.

Core Idea
Even what reason can know needs revelation to be known well.

Nasafi addresses a subtle point: if reason can know that some acts are obligatory and others impossible, why is revelation needed? He explains that there is an intense need for two reasons. One reason is specific to some rational beings, based on their varying intellectual capacities and preoccupations. In this passage, he begins to detail the first reason by recalling a previous discussion about the faith of the imitator and the division of people into three categories. Although the passage is cut off, it establishes a key principle about the relationship between reason and revelation.

Guided Path
1

Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Nasafi poses the question and begins to answer it.
The passage is incomplete, but the core argument is clear.

The passage states there are two reasons for the need of revelation. Find where Nasafi introduces them: one specific to some rational people, and one general.

The first reason refers to a previous discussion about the faith of the imitator and divides people into three categories. Note the description of the first category (sharp minds, devoted to reflection) and the beginning of the second (sound intellects, occupied with interests).
The third category is missing due to the cut-off.

The lesson focuses on the available content.

Source Text
