Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that empirical knowledge can arise solely from human reason and sense experience.
Correction: The text argues that many critical sciences (medicine, astronomy, etc.) require divine revelation because they are not discoverable by unaided human faculties.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is knowledge of the natures of substances like foods, poisons, and medicines considered a proof of prophethood?
A) Because it can be learned by anyone through experimentation.
B) Because human senses and intellect cannot attain it without a teacher, and the chain ends with a divine source.
C) Because ancient doctors like Hippocrates were prophets.
D) Because substances are created by humans.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of a divine teacher for specialised knowledge proves that prophethood must have been established in the past through revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one skill or piece of knowledge you possess that you did not figure out on your own—you learned it from someone. Who taught you? And who taught them? Trace it back as far as you can. Does it not ultimately lead back to God? Now, how does that chain of teaching make you responsible to use that knowledge for His sake?
?
The passage argues that even language itself must have been revealed by God. Every time you speak, you are using a gift whose ultimate origin is divine. When was the last time you thanked God for the ability to communicate? What would change in your life if you consciously remembered that your words are a trust from Him?
?
The proof shows that human reason alone is not enough to attain certain crucial knowledge. How does that humble your intellectual pride? In what area of your life are you still relying on your own understanding instead of seeking guidance from God's revelation? What step will you take today to surrender that area to Him?

Prophethood

The Necessity of Revelation for Human Knowledge
Al-Nasafi argues that knowledge of various sciences requires divine revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Nasafi argues that diverse sciences—from language to warfare—cannot be attained by reason alone and require divine revelation.

Core Idea
Reason cannot reach the sciences of life; revelation is necessary.

Al-Nasafi presents a sweeping argument: many bodies of knowledge that humans rely on daily, such as language, trade routes, and animal training, are beyond the capacity of unaided reason and sensory experience. He points to the tradition of Hermes/Idris and lists numerous examples to show that such knowledge must come from divine revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the list of sciences Al-Nasafi enumerates.
Pay attention to the phrase 'هذا من أبين الدلائل' which marks his key evidence.
2
Identify the central claim
Al-Nasafi claims that knowledge of these sciences is only possible through divine instruction (توقيف). Find where he states this directly.
3
Examine the examples
Review each science mentioned: letters, clothing, animal training, trade, languages, etc. Ask yourself: Could humans have discovered these without revelation?
Think especially about language: how could the first convention occur without a prior language?
4
Understand the reasoning
The argument is: these sciences cannot be attained by senses or intellect (الحواس أو العقل), so they must be from the All-Knowing.
Connect this to the broader Islamic doctrine of prophethood.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the implications: human knowledge is not self-sufficient; we depend on revelation for foundational sciences.
This counters the idea that human reason alone can achieve all knowledge.

When they were asked about that, they said: the beginning of knowledge of it is from Hermes the wise, and his spirit ascended to heaven, and it was in the kingdom of the heavens, and he knew all that by witnessing. And some of those who claimed knowledge of realities claimed that Hermes is the name of Idris (peace be upon him) among the people of the Maghrib in a science they know by this name, and the historians claim that the science of the stars was revealed to Idris (peace be upon him). And this is among the clearest evidences that all that was established by way of revelation from Allah Almighty. Likewise, the knowledge of letters by which livelihood is sustained, and the making of clothing and shelter and protection from heat and cold. And likewise, the knowledge of how to train difficult beasts, which is not known by reflection for what benefit they were created and in what way they can be trained, subdued, and their wild and shy ones tamed. And likewise, the knowledge of types of trades by which people have no religion or worldly life except through them. And likewise, the knowledge of what distinguishes their needs in the lands is only established by divine instruction due to the incapacity of intellects and senses to grasp that. And likewise, the knowledge of routes of lands, and likewise, knowledge of languages, since there is no way to convention except after the establishment of knowledge of a language by which the knowledge of that language enables reaching a convention on another language, since there is no access to that except after the conveners know what is in the conscience of others. Therefore, there must be a language established by revelation. And likewise, the knowledge of the aspects of causes of reproduction, and knowledge of how to raise young ones, and knowledge of managing the nutrition of what has no speech. And likewise, knowledge of magic and changing the essences of things by types of treatments. And sciences of fighting enemies. So the establishment of knowledge of these things, with the negation [؟] of attaining that by any of the senses or intellect, is evidence that knowledge of all of them is divine instruction from the All-Knowing, the All-Aware, from whom nothing is absent and no secret is hidden, and that is by revelation to whom He chooses for His message. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is an argument that many forms of knowledge cannot be attained by reason or senses alone and must be divinely revealed. It begins by mentioning a claim about Hermes/Idris and then states that this is clear evidence that such knowledge comes by revelation. Then it lists various types of knowledge: letters, clothing, housing, taming animals, trade, distinguishing needs of different lands, routes, languages, reproduction, child rearing, feeding animals, magic, changing substances, and warfare. The conclusion is that since these cannot be obtained by senses or intellect, they must be tawqif (divine instruction) from Allah, the All-Knowing, who reveals to His chosen messengers.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sciences like trade, child rearing, and language are developed purely through human trial and error without divine help.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that such sciences cannot be known by reason or senses alone; they require divine instruction (tawqif) from Allah.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi demonstrates that numerous fundamental sciences depend on divine revelation, proving that human reason is insufficient for complete knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on language every day to think, communicate, and connect with others. If even this basic tool is a gift from Allah through revelation, how does that change the way you use your tongue? Will you repent for every idle word or gossip you have spoken, knowing that speech itself is a divine trust?
?
Al-Nasafi lists sciences like farming, trade, and raising children as knowledge that cannot be fully attained without revelation. Is there an area of your life where you have relied solely on your own intellect and ignored Allah's guidance? What practical step can you take today to seek His instruction in that matter?
?
The passage shows that even the most practical human achievements—clothing, shelter, animal training—are ultimately from Allah. When you enjoy these blessings, do you feel a sense of gratitude and humility, or do you take them for granted as human inventions? How can you turn your daily routines into acts of worship, acknowledging their divine origin?

Prophethood

Miracles as Proof of Prophethood and Tawatur
Al-Nasafi presents miracles as proofs of prophethood and explains transmission via tawatur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how miracles serve as proofs of prophethood and how their transmission through mutawatir reports yields certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Miracles are God's signs that confirm prophets, and tawatur guarantees their authenticity.

Al-Nasafi explains that miracles (mu'jizat) are the primary evidence for prophethood. He lists examples from various prophets and then discusses how later generations know about these miracles. The key is that these reports are transmitted by many independent chains (tawatur), making collusion on a lie impossible, so we gain certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Nasafi structures his argument: first stating the claim, then giving examples, then explaining transmission.
The passage ends mid-sentence; focus on the presented logic.
2
Identify the main claim and examples
What is the main claim? (Miracles prove prophethood). List the miracles mentioned and note they 'break natural habits' (nāqiḍah lil-'ādāt).
Examples include the she-camel from rock, fire cooling for Ibrahim, etc.
3
Understand the transmission modes
How do contemporaries know? (Direct observation). How do later generations know? (Mutawatir report). What does mutawatir produce? (Necessary knowledge, 'ilm ḍarūrī).
Tawatur is a chain of so many narrators that lying is impossible.
4
Reflect on the impossibility of collusion
Why can't the narrators collude on a lie? Because they are diverse in inclinations, opinions, and natures, making agreement on a falsehood impossible.
This argument safeguards the reliability of prophetic reports.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعجزات دليل لثبوت الرسالة] ثم الدليل على ثبوت الرسالة لبعض الرسل على التعيين ظهور المعجزات الناقضات للعادات على أيدي كثير منهم كخروج الناقة من الصخر، وصيرورة النار بردًا وسلامًا على إبراهيم، وقلب العصا حية، وتسخير الريح والجن والشياطين وتسبيح الجبال، وإلانة الحديد، وإحياء الموتى، وحنين الأسطوانة وشكاية الناقة، وكلام الجمل المسموم ، ونبع الماء من بين الأصابع وغير ذلك مما هي أدلة صدق إدعاء هم الرسالة على ما بينا من كيفية وجه الدلالة. ثم ثبوت ذلك لأهل زمانهم بالمشاهدة ولمن بعدهم بالخبر المتواتر الذي يوجب العلم الضروري، كما أثبت ذلك بالبلدان النائية والأمم الخالية، ولا مجال للتهمة فيه لا بالفسق ولا بالانتفاع لامتناع إجماع الناس الذين لا يتصور تواطؤهم على الكذب على خبر واحد هو في نفسه كذب، لاستحالة اجتماع دواعي الكذب أناس مختلفين جبلوا على أهواء مختلفة، وآراء مشتتة، وهمم متفرقة، وطبائع
Miracles are proof for the establishment of prophethood. Then the proof of the establishment of prophethood for some messengers specifically is the appearance of miracles that break natural habits at the hands of many of them, such as the emergence of the she-camel from the rock, the fire becoming cool and safety for Ibrahim, the turning of the staff into a snake, the subjugation of the wind and jinn and devils, the glorification of the mountains, the softening of iron, the reviving of the dead, the groaning of the pillar, the complaint of the camel, the speech of the poisoned camel, the flowing of water from between the fingers, and other things that are proofs of the truthfulness of their claim to prophethood, as we have explained the manner of the indication. Then its establishment for the people of their time by direct observation, and for those after them by successive report (khabar mutawatir) that produces necessary knowledge, just as that is established in distant lands and past nations. And there is no room for accusation in it, neither by sin nor by benefit, because it is impossible for a consensus of people whose collusion on lying cannot be imagined, for one report that is itself a lie, due to the impossibility of the motives for lying coming together in people of diverse inclinations, scattered opinions, divergent aspirations, and varied natures.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines miracles as proofs of prophethood. It begins with a heading that miracles are proof of the establishment of prophethood. Then it states the proof: the appearance of miracles that break natural habits at the hands of many prophets, listing examples: the she-camel from rock for Salih, fire becoming cool for Ibrahim, staff turning into snake for Musa, wind and jinn subjugated for Sulayman, mountains glorifying for Dawud, iron softening, reviving dead for Isa, the pillar groaning, the camel complaining, the poisoned camel speaking, water flowing between fingers for Muhammad, etc. These are proofs of their truthful claim to prophethood. Then it explains the transmission: for contemporaries, it is by direct observation; for later generations, by mutawatir report that yields necessary (self-evident) knowledge. Such tawatur is immune from accusation of sin or interest because it is impossible for a large group of diverse people to collude on a lie, given their different inclinations, opinions, and dispositions.

Key Terms
معجزات
رسالة
خبر متواتر
علم ضروري
ناقضة للعادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are just stories that cannot be verified.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that the mutawatir transmission makes them as certain as direct observation.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, why does the mutawatir report of miracles yield necessary (ضروري) knowledge?
A) Because all people agree on them.
B) Because the sheer number of narrators makes collusion on a lie impossible.
C) Because the prophets themselves wrote them down.
D) Because the miracles are still happening today.

Key Takeaway
Miracles are divine confirmations of prophethood, and their transmission by tawatur assures their truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about a miracle like the water flowing from the Prophet's fingers, do you accept it as certain truth or do you feel a hint of doubt? What would change in your faith if you truly internalized that tawatur makes such reports as reliable as if you had seen it yourself?
?
You trust many things based on widespread reports—news events, historical facts, scientific claims. Why, then, do you sometimes treat reports of miracles with more skepticism? Is it because you assume they cannot happen, or because you haven't examined the strength of their transmission? Challenge yourself to investigate the chains of transmission for at least one miracle mentioned in this lesson.
?
If someone told you that the Prophet's miracles are just stories exaggerated over time, how would you respond using al-Nasafi's argument about the impossibility of collusion among diverse narrators? Would your response be merely academic, or would it affect how you live your daily life as a believer?

The Doubt against Miracles
Presents a skeptic's doubt against prophetic miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the skeptic's argument that miracles cannot serve as evidence for prophethood because they either conform to nature or are impossible.

Core Idea
The skeptic claims miracles are either natural or impossible — neither proves prophethood.

Before we can appreciate the refutation, we must first understand the doubt itself. The skeptic argues that if a miracle occurs naturally, it is no miracle, and if it defies nature, it is impossible — thus prophethood cannot be established by such events. This lesson presents that doubt as a stepping stone to the defense of prophetic miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the two alternatives the skeptic poses: if the event is natural or unnatural.
The skeptic uses a dichotomy: either within custom or beyond it.
2
Analyze the logical structure
Recognize that the doubt is built on two premises: no evidence from natural events, and no possibility of supernatural events. Consider why the skeptic thinks nature cannot change.
This reflects a naturalist assumption.
3
Reflect on the weakness
The author calls this speech feeble. Think about why someone might find this argument weak even before the refutation.
The author uses strong language to indicate the argument's absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما شبهة من زعم أن لا ثبوت للرسالة إلا بالدليل ولا ثبوت للدليل، فإنه لو كان خارجًا على ما عليه العادة ومجرى الطبيعة فلا دليل فيه، وإن خرج عن العادة ومجرى الطبيعة فهو محال إذ لا تغير للطبائع. فكلام واه لو تفوه به مجنون لتعجب منه، وذلك لما مر أن كل ذلك معاينه من لا يجوز خفاء أمره عليهم لكثرتهم، فكان ما أنكروه من الخروج عن الطبيعة بلا حجة شبهة اعتمدوا عليها ثابتا بالحس الذي هو أوضح طريق العلم وأعلى سبل المعرفة فكان إنكارهم مردودا، وجواز انقلاب الطبيعة ثابتا. يحققه أنهم لو اعتمدوا في ذلك على دليل يتراءى أنه معقول ولا يهتدي بالعقل إلى كيفية رده كان ذلك مردودًا لما أن الدليل العقلي يوجب العلم الاستدلالي والخطأ على الاستدلالي جائز ممكن، وما أنكروه ثابت بالحس الموجب للعلم الضروري الذي لا يجوز عليه الخطأ والغلط، ولابد من رد أحدهما ورد ما يجوز عليه الخطأ والغلط أولى من رد ما لا يجوز عليه ذلك. يحققه أن معرفة ثبوت ما أنكروه ثابت بالحس فكان بطلان دليلهم بالحس، وكل دليل عرف بطلانه ضرورة كان باطلاً أو كان شبهة لا دليلاً فكيف وهم لم يبنوا إنكارهم على شبهة.
As for the doubt of one who claims that prophethood is not established except by evidence and evidence is not established: for if it (the miracle) occurs according to custom and the course of nature, then there is no evidence in it; and if it departs from custom and the course of nature, then it is impossible because natures do not change. So it is feeble speech; if a madman uttered it, one would be amazed at him. That is because, as has passed, all that (miracle) is witnessed by people whose affair it is not possible to conceal from them due to their multitude; thus what they denied of departure from nature, without a proof, is a doubt they relied upon, while it (the departure) is established by sense (al-hiss), which is the clearest path of knowledge and the highest means of cognition. So their denial is rejected, and the permissibility of change of nature is established. He confirms it: if they relied in that upon an argument that appears to be rational and the intellect cannot find a way to refute it, that would still be rejected because rational argument yields speculative knowledge (al-ilm al-istidlali), and error in speculative knowledge is possible and permissible, while what they denied is established by sense which yields necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri) in which error and mistake are not possible. It is necessary to reject one of them, and rejecting that in which error and mistake are possible is more appropriate than rejecting that in which that is not possible. He confirms it: the knowledge of the establishment of what they denied is established by sense, so the invalidity of their argument is established by sense; and every argument known to be invalid necessarily is invalid, or it is a doubt not an argument. So how (could it be otherwise) while they did not base their denial on a doubt?
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents a doubt (shubha) and its refutation. The doubt states that prophethood cannot be established because neither natural nor miraculous events can serve as evidence: natural events are not evidence, and miracles are impossible since natures do not change. The author refutes this by first calling such speech feeble, then explaining that the miraculous events are witnessed by so many people that their concealment is impossible. The denial of such events is based on a false assumption, while the events themselves are established by sense perception (al-hiss), which is the clearest path to knowledge. The author then argues that even if the deniers had a rational argument against the miracle, that rational argument yields only speculative knowledge (al-ilm al-istidlali) which is subject to error, whereas sense perception yields necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri) which is not subject to error. When there is a conflict, it is more appropriate to reject the fallible (rational argument) than the infallible (sensory knowledge). Moreover, the occurrence of the miracle is known by sense, so their rational argument is disproven by sense. Any argument known to be invalid necessarily is either false or merely a doubt. Therefore the deniers' position is groundless.

Key Terms
شبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That miracles are impossible because nature cannot change
Correction: The author will argue that nature can change, as witnessed by many.

Checkpoint
According to the doubt, what happens if a miracle departs from the course of nature?
A) It provides evidence
B) It is impossible
C) It is natural
D) It is uncertain

Key Takeaway
The skeptic denies the possibility of miracles by arguing that any event is either natural (not evidence) or supernatural (impossible).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself demanding that Allah's existence or a prophet's truth be proven in a way that assumes nature never changes? How does this skeptic's argument reveal a hidden assumption in your own heart?
?
Imagine someone close to you says 'I would believe if I saw a miracle, but that's impossible.' How would you, after reading this lesson, gently expose the flaw in their thinking while respecting their doubt?
?
If you truly believe that Allah can break the laws of nature, why do you sometimes still expect life to follow predictable patterns? Where in your life do you need to trust His power over your habits and routines?

Refutation through Sensory Certainty
Refutes the doubt by prioritizing sensory certainty over rational inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sensory perception of miracles is superior to rational arguments against them.

Core Idea
Sensory knowledge is necessary and infallible; rational knowledge is speculative and fallible.

Having presented the doubt, the author now mounts a powerful refutation. He argues that the miracles were witnessed by multitudes, providing sensory certainty. Moreover, even if the skeptics had a rational argument against miracles, sensory knowledge takes precedence because it is necessary and infallible. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic from 'فكلام واه' to the end. Follow the author's argument step by step.
Note the shift from dismissing the doubt to establishing sensory certainty.
2
Distinguish the two types of knowledge
Identify where the author contrasts speculative knowledge (علم استدلالي) and necessary knowledge (علم ضروري). Understand why necessary knowledge is considered superior.
This is a key epistemological principle.
3
Apply the conflict resolution
The author argues that when sensory and rational knowledge conflict, the fallible rational must be rejected. See how he applies this to the miracle example.
The miracles are known by sense, so any rational objection must yield.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Distinguishing Miracles from Sorcery and Jugglery
Nasafi refutes the objection that miracles are like sorcery and juggling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's refutation of the claim that miracles are like sorcery, and the key distinctions he makes between miracles and deceptive acts.

Core Idea
Miracles withstand scrutiny, while sorcery and jugglery are exposed as illusion.

Nasafi presents a common objection against miracles: that they are merely clever tricks like sorcery or jugglery. In this passage, he refutes this objection by showing that miracles are fundamentally different in nature—they grow stronger under investigation, whereas deceptive acts dissolve. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage ends abruptly—Nasafi's full argument may continue, but the core refutation is clear.
2
Identify the objection and the refutation
Find where Nasafi states the opponent's claim (that miracles are within the power of sorcerers) and then his response ('corrupt speech').
The objector concedes that God can change customs but argues that it doesn't prove prophethood because sorcerers do similar things.
3
List the distinguishing features
Note the three features Nasafi uses to differentiate miracles from sorcery and jugglery: 1) jugglery weakens upon investigation, 2) miracles strengthen, 3) sorcery involves polytheism and impurity.
These differences show that miracles are not deceptive and therefore valid as proofs of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة والرد عليها] وأما من يزعم أن قلب العادة والطبيعة جائز ، غير أن ذلك أيضا في مقدور السحرة والمشعبذة . فكلام فاسد، إذ المشعبذة والمخرفة يزداد ضعفًا واضمحلالاً لدى البحث عنهما والتأمل فيهما، إذ المخرفة تمويه محض، والشعبذة مبنية على سفل أعين الناظرين بشيء ثم إخراج غيره وعلى المهارة في خفة اليد. والمعجزة تزداد عند البحث والتأمل وكادة وعلى مر الأيام ثباتاً ودواما. والسحر من علائق الكلمات المؤلفة من الشرك الخالص والكفر المحض والتعمد لتفخيم الشياطين. ثم في الأعم الأغلب يظهر على أيدي الحيض من النساء وذوي القذارة والنجاسة من الرجال، في أوقات ومواضع لا يجري فيها ذكر الله تعالى، وإذا وافقه
A specious argument and its refutation: As for one who claims that the transformation of custom and nature is permissible, but that also lies within the power of sorcerers and jugglers—this is corrupt speech. For jugglery and trickery increase in weakness and dissolution upon investigation and reflection. For trickery is pure illusion, and jugglery is based on lowering the eyes of the beholders with something and then bringing out something else, and on skill in hand dexterity. The miracle increases upon investigation and reflection in strength and over the days in permanence and duration. Sorcery is among the connections of words composed of pure polytheism and absolute disbelief and the deliberate magnification of devils. Then, in the vast majority, it appears at the hands of menstruating women and those of filth and impurity among men, at times and places where the remembrance of God is not mentioned, and if it coincides [with...]
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objector claims that the transformation of nature (inqilāb al-ʿāda wa l-ṭabīʿa) is possible, but it is also within the power of sorcerers (saḥara) and jugglers (mushaʿbidha). Nasafi refutes this as corrupt speech. He explains that jugglery (shuʿbadha) and trickery (mukharrafa) increase in weakness and dissolution when investigated—they are pure illusion and sleight of hand. In contrast, a miracle (muʿjiza) increases in strength and permanence over time when examined. He then distinguishes sorcery (siḥr) as based on words of polytheism and disbelief, magnifying devils, and often performed by impure individuals in impure circumstances. The passage is incomplete, but the core refutation is clear: miracles are qualitatively different from sorcery and jugglery, as they are not deceptive and withstand scrutiny.

Key Terms
شبهة
سحر
شعبذة
معجزة
قلب العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that miracles are just like sorcery or juggling tricks.
Correction: Nasafi shows that miracles are fundamentally different: they withstand scrutiny and increase in strength, while sorcery and jugglery are deceptive and weaken when investigated.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what happens to a miracle upon investigation?
A) It weakens and disappears
B) It increases in strength and permanence
C) It becomes confusing
D) It is exposed as trickery

Key Takeaway
Miracles are distinct from sorcery and jugglery because they are not deceptive; they grow stronger under scrutiny and are associated with divine purity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals that miracles strengthen under scrutiny while deception fades. Have you ever held a belief about your faith that seemed impressive but crumbled when you examined it deeply? What was your response? Will you commit to investigating your own certainties with honesty?
?

?
The passage contrasts the enduring firmness of divine miracles with the fleeting trick of jugglery. In your daily worship, do you seek the lasting substance of sincere devotion, or are you content with outward show that impresses people but fades? What one step can you take today to purify your intentions?

The Rational and Moral Foundation of Prophethood
Defense of prophetic teachings and introduction to proof of Muhammad's prophethood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that prophetic teachings are inherently rational and moral, and that prophethood is proven through miracles that break natural patterns.

Core Idea
Prophetic teachings are pure wisdom and reason, not impossibilities.

The author begins by refuting the accusation that prophets brought impossible things. He argues that their teachings are pure wisdom, clear truth, and pure rightness, even without miracles. He then outlines a method for proving Muhammad's prophethood through miraculous events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the strong refutation at the beginning.
2
Identify the refutation and affirmation
Find where the author refutes the claim that prophets brought impossibilities and then affirms what they actually brought.
3
List the four categories of prophetic teachings
The author categorizes teachings into four types: worship and gratitude, noble character, excellent policies, and interpersonal courtesy. Write them down.
These show the moral and social value of prophetic teachings.
4
Understand the role of pure reason in creed
Notice that the author says creedal matters are what pure reason necessitates. This is a key argument that prophetic teachings are rational.
This counters the misconception that faith is blind.
5
See the transition to proving Muhammad's prophethood
The author shifts to a new section, stating that after establishing prophethood in general, we specify Muhammad through his miracles like the staff turning into a serpent.
This shows the method of proof.

Source Text

وما زعمت الفرقة الأخيرة : أن الرسل أتوا بما هو محال على ما حكينا، قول باطل ودعوى فاسدة، بل أتوا بما هو الحكمة المحضة والحق البين والصواب الخالص ولولا التأيد بالبرهان لكان تعاطي ما أتوا به ودعوا إليه حقا وصوابًا إذ بعض ذلك من باب من باب التقرب إلى الصانع وأداء شكر ما أسدى إليهم، وبعضه من مكارم الأخلاق، وبعضه من السياسات الفاضلة التي لا قيام للعالم ولا سكون للدهماء إلا بها، وبعضه من باب المجاملة وحسن العشرة والمعاملة. فأما ما أتوا به من الأبواب الاعتقادية فمما يوجبه العقل الصريح، ولا يطلق على ما يضاده من الاعتقاد بل يشهد ببطلان كل ما وراءه من العقائد، وفساد ما يقابله من الآراء والديانات (۳) على ما نكشف عن حقيقة ذلك إذا انتهينا إلى إثبات نبوة نبينا محمد عليه السلام - إن شاء الله تعالى. إثبات رسالة سيدنا محمد ] ثم بعد قيام (٤) الدليل على صحة الرسالة وثبوتها فيما مضى من الجملة وتعين من قامت على يده المعجزات الناقضات للعادة كقلب العصا حية، وتفجير عيون
And what the last sect claimed – that the messengers brought what is impossible according to what we have narrated – is a false statement and a corrupt claim. Rather, they brought nothing but pure wisdom, clear truth, and pure rightness. And were it not for divine support with proof, the undertaking of what they brought and called to would be right and correct. Indeed, some of it is from the category of drawing near to the Creator and performing gratitude for what He bestowed upon them; some of it is from noble character; some of it is from excellent policies without which the world cannot stand nor the masses find tranquility; and some of it is from the category of courtesy and good companionship and dealing. As for what they brought of matters of belief, it is what pure reason (al-ʿaql al-ṣarīḥ) necessitates, and it does not give approval to what opposes it of belief, rather it testifies to the falsity of all beliefs besides it, and the corruption of what opposes it of opinions and religions, as we will uncover the reality of that when we reach the proof of the prophethood of our Prophet Muhammad, peace be upon him – if God Most High wills. [Proving the Mission of Our Master Muhammad] Then, after the proof of the validity of the prophetic mission and its establishment in the past in general, and the specification of the one at whose hand the miracle-breaking-customs occurred, such as the turning of the staff into a serpent, and the gushing forth of springs
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the content of prophetic teachings against the charge that they include impossibilities. The author first refutes the claim as false and corrupt. He then affirms that prophets brought pure wisdom (ḥikma maḥḍa), clear truth, and pure rightness. He argues that even without miraculous support, their teachings would be inherently right and good. He categorizes their teachings into four types: worship and gratitude to the Creator, noble character traits, excellent political policies necessary for social order, and interpersonal courtesy and good conduct. Regarding creedal matters (al-abwāb al-iʿtiqādiyya), he asserts that they are what pure reason (al-ʿaql al-ṣarīḥ) necessitates, and they invalidate all opposing beliefs. The author then transitions to a new section titled 'Proving the Mission of Our Master Muhammad', where he outlines the method: after establishing the general validity of prophethood and that miracles occur only for true prophets, one specifies Muhammad's prophethood through his specific miracles, such as the staff turning into a serpent and springs gushing forth – though the text is cut off here.

The Logical Possibility of Miracles: Al-Nasafi's Criterion
Al-Nasafi states the condition for a claim to be possible and asserts that miracles are possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Nasafi's criterion for when a lack of proof does not imply divine incapability, and why miracles are therefore possible.

Core Idea
Miracles are possible because the claim of prophethood is not inherently impossible.

Al-Nasafi addresses a key objection: if a miracle is unusual, does its occurrence imply God's inability to provide proof? He argues that the real question is whether the claim itself is logically possible. Only when a claim is possible does the lack of proof become problematic. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source text once, then follow with the English translation. Notice the conditional structure Al-Nasafi uses.
Pay attention to the terms 'possible' (mumkin) and 'impossible' (mumtaniʿ).
2
Identify the condition for implication
Al-Nasafi says that lack of proof only implies equalization or incapability if the claim is possible in itself. Find where he states this condition.
The phrase 'إنما تثبت التسوية أو العجز عن التفرقة إذا كانت الدعوى في نفسها ممكنة غير ممتنعة' is key.
3
Understand the exception for impossible claims
When a claim is inherently impossible, failure to prove it does not lead to the negative implications. Reflect on why this makes sense logically.
An impossible claim cannot be proven, so its unproven status is not surprising.
4
Apply the principle to miracles
Al-Nasafi concludes that miracles (nawāqid al-ʿādah) are possible because the claim of prophethood is not impossible. See how he connects the general rule to this specific case.
This defends the possibility of prophethood against skeptics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التسوية بين (۱) الحق والباطل أو إلى تعجيز الباري عن إقامة الدليل على ما من الدعاوي والتفرقة بين الحق منها والباطل، وإنما تثبت التسوية أو العجز عن التفرقة إذا كانت الدعوى في نفسها ممكنة غير ممتنعة، فأما عند امتناعها (۲) فلا يؤدي إلى ذلك، ولهذا كان قيام الناقض للعادة على يدي المتأله ممكنا غير ممتنع، والله الموفق.
The equalization between truth and falsehood or the incapacitation of the Creator from establishing proof for some of the claims and the distinction between truth and falsehood among them. And equalization or incapability of distinction is only established when the claim in itself is possible, not impossible. As for when it is impossible, that does not lead to that. And for this reason, the occurrence of something that breaks the custom (nāqiḍ al-ʿādah) at the hands of the mutaʾallih is possible, not impossible. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defines the condition for a claim to be considered proof-worthy. Al-Nasafi argues that if a claim is inherently impossible (mumtaniʿ), then the failure to prove it does not imply equalization or helplessness. But when a claim is possible, then the lack of proof would imply equalization. He applies this to miracles (nawāqid al-ʿādah): they are possible because the claim of prophethood is not impossible. Therefore, the occurrence of miracles is possible, and God enables them.

Key Terms

تعجيز الباري
الدعوى
ممكنة
ممتنعة
الناقض للعادة
المتأله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a miracle is unusual, it must be impossible.
Correction: Al-Nasafi clarifies that possibility is not determined by rarity but by inherent logical possibility. Miracles are possible because the claim of prophethood is not contradictory.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, when does the failure to prove a claim not imply equalization or divine incapability?
A) When the claim is inherently impossible
B) When the claim is possible but not proven
C) When the claimant is not a prophet
D) When the claim is about miracles

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi establishes that miracles are logically possible because the claim of prophethood is not inherently impossible, and thus the lack of alternative proof does not indicate divine failure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter something that defies your normal experience, do you immediately dismiss it as impossible, or do you first consider whether it could be a sign from God? How does Al-Nasafi's logical criterion challenge your natural impulse to equate rarity with impossibility? Commit today to examining the possibility of divine signs before rejecting them.
?
Think of a time you doubted a truth claim because it seemed 'unusual.' Did you confuse your own limited experience with logical impossibility? How can this lesson reshape your attitude toward the miraculous? Make a pledge to treat the unusual as a potential opening for faith rather than a reason for denial.
?
Al-Nasafi argues that if a claim is possible, the lack of proof does not discredit it. Have you ever demanded absolute proof for a religious truth while accepting less for everyday matters? What does this asymmetry say about your heart? Resolve to apply the same standard of possibility to divine matters as you do to worldly matters.

The Prophets Brought Wisdom, Not Impossibilities
Al-Nasafi refutes the group claiming prophets brought impossibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi refutes the claim that prophets brought impossible things and explains the rational and beneficial nature of prophetic teachings.

Core Idea
Prophetic teachings are always wise, true, and rationally sound—never impossible.

This passage confronts a direct objection: some groups argued that prophets brought impossible things. Al-Nasafi decisively rejects this as false. He then explains what prophets actually brought—pure wisdom, truth, and correctness—and categorizes their teachings to show their inherent beneficence and rationality. This sets the stage for a deeper defense of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow. Notice the strong refutation at the beginning.
The passage is a direct response to an opposing group.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that prophets brought impossibilities (muḥāl). Al-Nasafi calls this false and corrupt.
3
Examine the positive description
Al-Nasafi lists what prophets actually brought: pure wisdom, clear truth, and pure correctness. He then categorizes the teachings into four practical areas and one doctrinal area.
The four practical categories are worship, morals, policies, and courtesy.
4
Understand the role of reason
Doctrinal matters are what pure reason necessitates. Reason does not allow opposite beliefs but testifies to their invalidity.
This shows that prophetic beliefs are rationally binding.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how al-Nasafi's response refutes the claim and lays the foundation for proving prophethood through reason and benefit.
He promises a fuller exposition later.

And what the last group claimed—that the messengers brought what is impossible, according to what we have related—is a false statement and a corrupt claim. Rather, they brought what is pure wisdom, clear truth, and pure correctness. Were it not for being supported by proof, practicing what they brought and called to would be truth and correctness, since some of it is of the type of drawing near to the Maker and performing gratitude for what He bestowed upon them, and some of it is of noble morals, and some of it is of virtuous policies without which the world would not stand nor the common people be at rest, and some of it is of the type of courtesy and good companionship and dealings. As for what they brought of the doctrinal matters, it is what pure reason necessitates, and it does not permit its opposite of belief, rather it testifies to the invalidity of all beliefs besides it and the corruption of the opinions and religions that oppose it—as we will uncover the reality of that when we reach the proof of the prophethood of our Prophet Muhammad, peace be upon him, God willing.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The objection is from a group that claims the prophets brought impossible things. Al-Nasafi rejects this as false. He then explains that prophets brought wisdom, truth, and correctness. Their teachings fall into categories: some relating to worship and gratitude to God, some to moral virtues, some to social policies essential for order, and some to etiquette and good conduct. Regarding beliefs (iʿtiqādīyāt), they are what pure reason necessitates. Reason bears witness to the falsity of any opposite belief. He concludes by noting that he will later provide a detailed exposition when discussing the proof of Muhammad's prophethood.

Why Accept Muhammad's Prophethood Swiftly?
Al-Nasafi explains when rejection is rational and why Muhammad's claim passes the test.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Nasafi's rational argument that a claim should be accepted quickly if it is not impossible, rare, or unnecessary, and how this applies to Muhammad's prophethood.

Core Idea
Muhammad's prophethood is not strange; it is part of a continuous tradition demanding swift acceptance.

Al-Nasafi presents a preliminary argument before discussing specific miracles. He argues that a rational person should not reject a claim at first hearing unless it is impossible or unnecessary. Since Muhammad's message is continuous with previous prophets, it deserves immediate consideration. This lesson explores that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first context, then the rule, then application.
This is a rational argument, not a proof from miracles.
2
Identify the criteria for rejection
Find the conditions that would justify rejecting a claim at first hearing: impossibility, rarity, lack of need. Also notice the opposite is true for Muhammad.
The passage uses words like mumtaniʿ, nādir, mustaghghanī.
3
See how Muhammad's message fits
Al-Nasafi says Muhammad's message is not strange or far-fetched; it is continuous with custom and previous messengers. This means the criteria for rejection do not apply, so it should be accepted.
The Quranic verse is cited as proof.
4
Reflect on the rational principle
Consider how this principle can be applied to other claims. What does it tell us about how rational people should evaluate claims of prophethood?
This is a foundational principle for evaluating new claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إثبات رسالة سيدنا محمد ] ثم بعد قيام (٤) الدليل على صحة الرسالة وثبوتها فيما مضى من الجملة وتعين من قامت على يده المعجزات الناقضات للعادة كقلب العصا حية، وتفجير عيون الماء من الصخرة الصماء، وإبراء (۱) الأكمة والأبرص، وإحياء الموتى، وغير ذلك من الآيات للرسالة كموسى وعيسى وغيرهما من الأنبياء عليهم السلام. تصرف العناية إلى إثبات رسالة نبينا محمد بن عبد الله بن عبد المطلب بن هاشم بن عبد مناف لله فنقول وبالله التوفيق [ العاقل يسارع إلى الإذعان لرسالة محمد ]: إنا قبل أن نشتغل بإثبات أعلامه وآياته وأنواعها في أنفسها، ودلالة كل منها في نفسها على صحة ما أدعى من رسالته. نبين ما يستدل به العاقل المنصف على أن أمر رسالته كان أمرًا يلزم العاقل المسارعة إلى قبوله والتمسك به بأدنى دليل يقترن به، والتحامي عن الاشتغال برده والامتناع عن الإعراض عنه، وذلك أن الأمر إنما يقابل بالرد في أول الوهلة والإنكار له في ابتداء ما يسمع إذا كان أمرًا ممتنع الوجود مستحيل الثبوت أو نادرا في نفسه مستغربًا في ذاته ينبو عنه مكانه ولا يقبله زمانه، وكانت الغنية عنه متقررة والحاجة إليه منعدمة، فكان ما ادعى هو الله من الرسالة بمضادة ما بينا من الأمور، ومباينة ما ذكرنا من المعاني. رسالة سيدنا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم امتداد للرسالات قبله والزمن يطلبها ] فإن أمره عليه السلام لم (۲) يكن مستغربًا مستبعدًا، بل كان مستمرًا على العادة جاريًا على سنن الأمور المستفيضة، وما هذا سبيله لا ينبغي للعاقل أن يشتغل برده في أول الوهلة ، ولهذا علمه الله أن يحاج منكري أمره والمعرضين عن التأمل في صحة ما أتى به لقوله تعالى: ﴿ قُلْ مَا كُنتُ بِدْعَا مِنَ الرُّسُلِ ﴾ (۳)، وقوله
Establishing the message of our master Muhammad. Then, after the evidence is established for the correctness and confirmation of prophethood in general in the past, and the one at whose hands miracles breaking customs occurred—like the turning of the staff into a snake, the gushing of water from the hard rock, the healing of the blind and leprous, and the reviving of the dead, and other signs of prophethood, like Moses, Jesus, and other prophets, peace be upon them—attention is turned to establishing the message of our prophet Muhammad ibn ʿAbd Allāh ibn ʿAbd al-Muṭṭalib ibn Hāshim ibn ʿAbd Manāf. So we say, and with God is success: [The rational person hastens to acknowledge the message of Muhammad:] Indeed, before we engage in establishing his signs and miracles and their types in themselves, and the indication of each of them in itself of the correctness of what he claimed of his message, we clarify what the rational, fair-minded person deduces: that his matter was a matter that obliges the rational person to hasten to accept it and adhere to it with the slightest evidence connected to it, and to avoid engaging in rejecting it and refraining from turning away from it. That is because a claim is met with rejection at first glance and denial at first hearing only if it is a thing impossible in existence, impossible to be established, or rare in itself, strange in essence, that its place shuns it and its time does not accept it, and independence from it is established and need for it is non-existent. But the message he claimed, may God raise his rank, was contrary to the matters we have mentioned and different from the meanings we have stated. The message of our master Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, is an extension of the messages before him, and time demands it. For his affair, peace be upon him, was not strange or far-fetched, but rather continuous with custom, running according to the widespread course of affairs. And what is like this, it is not fitting for the rational person to engage in rejecting it at first glance. For this reason, God taught him to argue with those who deny his affair and turn away from reflecting on the correctness of what he brought, by His saying, 'Say: I am not a novelty among the messengers' (Quran 46:9), and His saying...
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a rational argument for the quick acceptance of Muhammad's prophethood. Al-Nasafi first sets the context: after establishing the general proof of prophethood through miracles, we turn to Muhammad specifically. He then makes a preliminary point before discussing individual miracles: a rational person should hasten to accept Muhammad's message with minimal evidence. Why? Because objections and denial at first hearing are only appropriate if the claim is impossible, rare, or unnecessary. Muhammad's claim is none of these. On the contrary, his message is continuous with the previous prophets; it is not strange or unprecedented. Rather, it follows the normal course of divine communication. Therefore, a rational person should not busy himself with rejection. Al-Nasafi cites Quran 46:9 as proof: Muhammad is not a novelty among messengers; he is one in a chain. The argument concludes that time demands such a message.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Muhammad's prophethood was a novel claim that demanded strong evidence before acceptance.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that it is not novel; it is an extension of previous prophethood, so it should be accepted with minimal evidence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, when is it rational to reject a claim at first hearing?
A) When the claim is about a new religion

Key Takeaway

The Rational Principle for Accepting Prophethood
Nasafi establishes a rational principle and applies it to Muhammad's prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Nasafi, a rational person should not reject a prophetic claim at first blush unless it is impossible, strange, or unnecessary, and that Muhammad's claim meets none of these criteria.

Core Idea
Prophethood claims are not automatically rejected; only impossible or strange ones are.

Before diving into the miracles of the Prophet, Nasafi establishes a crucial rational principle: not every claim deserves instant rejection. He argues that rejection is only warranted for claims that are impossible, extremely rare, or unnecessary. Since Muhammad's prophethood is none of these, the rational person must hasten to accept it even with minimal evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle
Read the first part where Nasafi states when a claim should be rejected at first blush. Identify the three conditions.
These conditions are: impossibility, strangeness, and lack of need.
2
Apply to Muhammad
Now read how Nasafi applies this principle to the Prophet's claim. Notice that he says it was 'not strange' but 'continuous according to custom'.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: What does this principle say about how we should approach claims of prophethood? Does it make rejection unreasonable here?
This connects to the learning objective.

إنا قبل أن نشتغل بإثبات أعلامه وآياته وأنواعها في أنفسها، ودلالة كل منها في نفسها على صحة ما أدعى من رسالته. نبين ما يستدل به العاقل المنصف على أن أمر رسالته كان أمرًا يلزم العاقل المسارعة إلى قبوله والتمسك به بأدنى دليل يقترن به، والتحامي عن الاشتغال برده والامتناع عن الإعراض عنه، وذلك أن الأمر إنما يقابل بالرد في أول الوهلة والإنكار له في ابتداء ما يسمع إذا كان أمرًا ممتنع الوجود مستحيل الثبوت أو نادرا في نفسه مستغربًا في ذاته ينبو عنه مكانه ولا يقبله زمانه، وكانت الغنية عنه متقررة والحاجة إليه منعدمة، فكان ما ادعى هو الله من الرسالة بمضادة ما بينا من الأمور، ومباينة ما ذكرنا من المعاني. رسالة سيدنا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم امتداد للرسالات قبله والزمن يطلبها. فإن أمره عليه السلام لم يكن مستغربًا مستبعدًا، بل كان مستمرًا على العادة جاريًا على سنن الأمور المستفيضة، وما هذا سبيله لا ينبغي للعاقل أن يشتغل برده في أول الوهلة، ولهذا علمه الله أن يحاج منكري أمره والمعرضين عن التأمل في صحة ما أتى به لقوله تعالى: ﴿ قُلْ مَا كُنتُ بِدْعَا مِنَ الرُّسُلِ ﴾، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَإِن مِنْ أُمَّةٍ إِلَّا خَلَا فِيهَا نَذِيرٌ ﴾، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلِكُلِّ قَوْمٍ هَادٍ ﴾ وقوله تعالى: ﴿ ثُمَّ أَرْسَلْنَا رُسُلَنَا تَترا ﴾، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ أَكَانَ لِلنَّاسِ عَجَبًا أَنْ أَوْحَيْنَا إِلَى رجلٍ مِّنْهُمْ أَنْ أَنذِرِ النَّاسَ ﴾ وكان مجيئه موافقا وقت الحاجة إليه، إذ كان في زمن فترة من الرسل ودروس من الملك، وطموس أعلام الحق وآثاره وانمحاء شعاره وستاره، مع جري عادة الله بمعاقبة أسباب الهداية عند زوال أهل الحق عن نهجه وزيغهم عن سبيله، وما هذا سبيله يجب المسارعة إلى قبوله ولا يطلق العقل الإعراض عن مثله، ولهذا قال الله تعالى: ﴿ قَدْ جَاءَكُمْ رَسُولُنَا يُبَيِّنُ لَكُمْ عَلَى فَتْرَةٍ مِّنَ الرُّسُلِ ﴾ وكان المنعوت إليهم بمحل الحاجة إليه، لخلاء جنسهم عن أسباب العلم ولهذا قال تعالى: ﴿ هُوَ الَّذِي بَعَثَ فِي الْأُمِينَ رَسُولًا مِّنْهُمْ ﴾ فكان ثبوته في أظهر الأماكن للخلق إذ هو واسطة ممالك الأرض، وهو موسم العرب قاطبة، ومتجر أهل الآفاق في كل سنة، وما يظهر من الأمور العظام والأنباء الجسام في مثل هذا المكان يكون انتشاره وظهور آثاره أسرع، وبلوغه إلى من بأطراف الأرض، وأقطارها أعجل وأوحى، وهذا أولى أمكنة الأرض وبقاعها، يكون من هو مبعوث إلى الناس كافة فيه قال الله: ﴿ وَكَذَلِكَ أَوْحَيْنَا إِلَيْكَ قُرْءَانَا عَرَبِيًّا لِتُنذِرَ أُمَّ الْقُرَى وَمَنْ حَوْهَا ﴾ ثم إن مجيئه كان عقيب تمنى القوم ذلك وإظهار الرغبة على ما قال الله تعالى
Before we occupy ourselves with establishing his signs and miracles and their types in themselves, and the indication of each of them in itself of the correctness of what he claimed of his message, we explain what the fair-minded rational person uses as evidence that his matter of his message was a matter that obliges the rational person to hasten to accept it and adhere to it with the slightest evidence associated with it, and to avoid occupying himself with rejecting it and refraining from turning away from it. That is because a matter is only met with rejection at first blush and denial at the beginning of what is heard if it is a matter of impossible existence, impossible to establish, or rare in itself, strange in itself, from which its place shrinks and its time does not accept it, and sufficiency from it is established and need for it is nonexistent. Thus what he, may God raise his mention, claimed of the message was contrary to the matters we have explained and different from the meanings we have mentioned. The message of our master Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, is an extension of the messages before him, and the time demands it. For his affair, peace be upon him, was not strange or remote; rather it was continuous according to custom, proceeding upon the course of widespread matters. And what is of this way, it is not fitting for the rational person to occupy himself with rejecting it at first blush. For this reason, God taught him to argue with those who deny his affair and those who turn away from reflecting on the correctness of what he brought, by His saying, the Most High: 'Say: I am not a novelty among the messengers' (46:9), and His saying, the Most High: 'And there was no nation but a warner had passed away in it' (35:24), and His saying, the Most High: 'And for every people there is a guide' (13:7), and His saying, the Most High: 'Then We sent Our messengers successively' (23:44), and His saying, the Most High: 'Has it been a wonder to people that We revealed to a man from among them: Warn the people?' (10:2). And his coming was in accordance with the time of need for him, because he was in a period of intermission of messengers and erosion of sovereignty and effacement of the signs of truth and its traces and obliteration of its emblem and covering, along with the customary practice of God in punishing the causes of guidance when the people of truth deviate from His path and stray from His way. And what is of this way, hastening to accept it is obligatory, and reason does not permit turning away from its like. For this reason God, the Most High, said: 'Indeed Our messenger has come to you making clear to you, after an interval of messengers' (5:19). And those addressed were in a place of need for him, because their kind was devoid of means of knowledge. For this reason He, the Most High, said: 'It is He who has sent among the unlettered a messenger from among themselves' (62:2). So his establishment was in the most apparent of places to creation, for it is the center of the kingdoms of the earth, and it is the meeting place of all the Arabs and the market of the people of distant horizons every year. And whatever appears of great matters and momentous news in such a place, its dissemination and the appearance of its traces are faster, and its reaching to those in the far ends of the earth and its regions is swifter and more rapid. And this is the most appropriate of the places of the earth and its regions for one who is sent to all people to be in it. God said: 'And thus We have revealed to you an Arabic Quran that you may warn the Mother of Cities and those around it' (42:7). Then his coming was after the people's wish for that and the manifestation of desire, according to what God, the Most High, said...
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a rational and scriptural argument for the necessity of accepting the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him) without initial rejection. It is a continuous argument consisting of three parts: first, a general principle about what kinds of claims should be rejected at first blush; second, an application showing that Muhammad's claim does not fall under that category; third, specific circumstances that make his coming timely and appropriate.

The author begins by stating his method: before proving the miracles and signs individually, he wants to establish a general rational principle that any fair-minded rational person can see. The principle is that a claim is rightly rejected immediately if it is impossible, or if it is so strange and unheard of that the time and place do not accept it, and there is no need for it. Since the claim of Muhammad's prophethood is none of these—it is not impossible, not strange in itself, given the continuous history of prophets before him—the rational person should hasten to accept it even with minimal evidence.

The author then supports this with several Quranic verses that show that Muhammad was not a novelty among messengers, that every nation had a warner, that messengers were sent consecutively, and that people should not be amazed at divine revelation to a man. These verses establish that prophethood is a customary divine practice.

Next, he argues that Muhammad's coming was at a time of great need: there was a period of intermission between prophets, signs of truth had faded, and the people were in need of guidance. God's custom is to send guidance when it is needed most. Therefore, reason itself requires acceptance, not rejection.

Finally, he points out the optimal location of his mission: Mecca, the center of the earth, the meeting place of all Arabs and international traders, ensuring rapid spread of his message. God chose the 'Mother of Cities' to warn all people. The passage ends with an incomplete statement about the people's desire for a prophet before his coming, but the argument is clear that everything about Muhammad's prophethood is rationally compelling and in line with divine custom.

Key Terms
رسالة
عاقل
مستغرب
العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any claim of prophethood should be met with initial skepticism until proven.
Correction: Nasafi argues that only claims that are impossible, strange, or unnecessary should be rejected initially. If a claim fits the pattern of divine custom, it should be accepted with minimal evidence.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what kind of claim should be rejected at first blush?
A) Any claim that seems unfamiliar.
B) A claim that is impossible, strange, or unnecessary.
C) A claim that is made by a non-relative.
D) A claim that is supported by miracles.

Key Takeaway
Rejection at first blush is only rational for impossible, strange, or unnecessary claims; Muhammad's prophethood was none of these.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you dismissed a religious claim or idea 'at first blush' because it seemed unfamiliar or challenging. Did your reaction align with Nasafi's principle, or were you quick to reject without fair consideration? What would it mean to instead 'hasten to accept' with the slightest evidence if the claim is not impossible or strange?
?
Nasafi says the rational person should not occupy himself with rejecting if the claim is continuous with custom. Are there areas of your faith—like the necessity of prophethood or the sending of messengers—that you take for granted rather than actively affirming? What would change if you treated them as matters requiring your urgent acceptance?
?
The passage argues that the 'time demands' Muhammad's message. Do you feel a personal urgency in your own life to respond to that message, or do you treat it as ancient history? How would your daily choices reflect the claim that prophethood is a living, present reality?

Prophethood
intermediate
5 min
Quranic Evidence and the Timing of Need
Nasafi cites Quranic verses and argues that Muhammad's prophethood was timely and necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Nasafi uses Quranic verses to show that prophethood is a continuous practice, and that Muhammad's coming was at a time of great need when guidance had faded.

Core Idea
Prophethood is a continuous custom; Muhammad came when humanity needed him most.

After establishing the rational principle, Nasafi supports his argument with Quranic verses that emphasize the continuity of prophethood. He also highlights that Muhammad's mission occurred during a 'period of intermission' when truth was scarce, making his arrival necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses
Nasafi cites five verses. Read each one and note how they support the idea that prophethood is not a novelty.
Verses: 46:9, 35:24, 13:7, 23:44, 10:2.
2
Identify the timing argument
Read the part about 'time of need' and 'intermission of messengers'. Understand why this makes acceptance obligatory.
The Prophet came after a long gap when guidance was fading.
3
Connect to rational obligation
How does this timing reinforce the earlier principle? Reason sees that a guide is needed, so rejecting is irrational.

What does Nasafi cite as evidence that prophethood is a continuous practice?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If every nation had a guide, and you have received the final guide, what obligation does that place on you to be a guide to others? How are you failing in that responsibility right now?

The Strategic Location of Mecca
Nasafi highlights the geographical advantages of Mecca for the Prophet's mission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Nasafi argues that Mecca was the optimal location for the Prophet's mission because it was a center of trade and pilgrimage, ensuring rapid dissemination.

Core Idea
Mecca's central location made it the perfect place for a universal message.

Nasafi further supports the rationality of accepting Muhammad's prophethood by pointing out that he was sent in Mecca, the 'Mother of Cities,' which was a hub for trade and pilgrimage. This ensured that his message would spread quickly to all corners of the earth.

Guided Path
1
Read the location argument
Nasafi describes Mecca as the 'most apparent of places' and the center of kingdoms. Understand why this is significant.
Mecca was a meeting place for Arabs and international traders annually.
2
See the Quranic evidence
He cites verse 42:7 about warning the Mother of Cities.
This shows the Quran itself affirms the centrality of Mecca.
3
Reflect on the wisdom
Why would Allah choose Mecca? Consider how this supports the claim that the message was meant for all people.
This ties back to the rational argument that the claim should be accepted.

إنا قبل أن نشتغل بإثبات أعلامه وآياته وأنواعها في أنفسها، ودلالة كل منها في نفسها على صحة ما أدعى من رسالته. نبين ما يستدل به العاقل المنصف على أن أمر رسالته كان أمرًا يلزم العاقل المسارعة إلى قبوله والتمسك به بأدنى دليل يقترن به، والتحامي عن الاشتغال برده والامتناع عن الإعراض عنه، وذلك أن الأمر إنما يقابل بالرد في أول الوهلة والإنكار له في ابتداء ما يسمع إذا كان أمرًا ممتنع الوجود مستحيل الثبوت أو نادرا في نفسه مستغربًا في ذاته ينبو عنه مكانه ولا يقبله زمانه، وكانت الغنية عنه متقررة والحاجة إليه منعدمة، فكان ما ادعى هو الله من الرسالة بمضادة ما بينا من الأمور، ومباينة ما ذكرنا من المعاني. رسالة سيدنا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم امتداد للرسالات قبله والزمن يطلبها. فإن أمره عليه السلام لم يكن مستغربًا مستبعدًا، بل كان مستمرًا على العادة جاريًا على سنن الأمور المستفيضة، وما هذا سبيله لا ينبغي للعاقل أن يشتغل برده في أول الوهلة، ولهذا علمه الله أن يحاج منكري أمره والمعرضين عن التأمل في صحة ما أتى به لقوله تعالى: ﴿ قُلْ مَا كُنتُ بِدْعَا مِنَ الرُّسُلِ ﴾، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَإِن مِنْ أُمَّةٍ إِلَّا خَلَا فِيهَا نَذِيرٌ ﴾، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلِكُلِّ قَوْمٍ هَادٍ ﴾ وقوله تعالى: ﴿ ثُمَّ أَرْسَلْنَا رُسُلَنَا تَترا ﴾، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ أَكَانَ لِلنَّاسِ عَجَبًا أَنْ أَوْحَيْنَا إِلَى رجلٍ مِّنْهُمْ أَنْ أَنذِرِ النَّاسَ ﴾ وكان مجيئه موافقا وقت الحاجة إليه، إذ كان في زمن فترة من الرسل ودروس من الملك، وطموس أعلام الحق وآثاره وانمحاء شعاره وستاره، مع جري عادة الله بمعاقبة أسباب الهداية عند زوال أهل الحق عن نهجه وزيغهم عن سبيله، وما هذا سبيله يجب المسارعة إلى قبوله ولا يطلق العقل الإعراض عن مثله، ولهذا قال الله تعالى: ﴿ قَدْ جَاءَكُمْ رَسُولُنَا يُبَيِّنُ لَكُمْ عَلَى فَتْرَةٍ مِّنَ الرُّسُلِ ﴾ وكان المنعوت إليهم بمحل الحاجة إليه، لخلاء جنسهم عن أسباب العلم ولهذا قال تعالى: ﴿ هُوَ الَّذِي بَعَثَ فِي الْأُمِينَ رَسُولًا مِّنْهُمْ ﴾ فكان ثبوته في أظهر الأماكن للخلق إذ هو واسطة ممالك الأرض، وهو موسم العرب قاطبة، ومتجر أهل الآفاق في كل سنة، وما يظهر من الأمور العظام والأنباء الجسام في مثل هذا المكان يكون انتشاره وظهور آثاره أسرع، وبلوغه إلى من بأطراف الأرض، وأقطارها أعجل وأوحى، وهذا أولى أمكنة الأرض وبقاعها، يكون من هو مبعوث إلى الناس كافة فيه قال الله: ﴿ وَكَذَلِكَ أَوْحَيْنَا إِلَيْكَ قُرْءَانَا عَرَبِيًّا لِتُنذِرَ أُمَّ الْقُرَى وَمَنْ حَوْهَا ﴾ ثم إن مجيئه كان عقيب تمنى القوم ذلك وإظهار الرغبة على ما قال الله تعالى
Before we occupy ourselves with establishing his signs and miracles and their types in themselves, and the indication of each of them in itself of the correctness of what he claimed of his message, we explain what the fair-minded rational person uses as evidence that his matter of his message was a matter that obliges the rational person to hasten to accept it and adhere to it with the slightest evidence associated with it, and to avoid occupying himself with rejecting it and refraining from turning away from it. That is because a matter is only met with rejection at first blush and denial at the beginning of what is heard if it is a matter of impossible existence, impossible to establish, or rare in itself, strange in itself, from which its place shrinks and its time does not accept it, and sufficiency from it is established and need for it is nonexistent. Thus what he, may God raise his mention, claimed of the message was contrary to the matters we have explained and different from the meanings we have mentioned. The message of our master Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, is an extension of the messages before him, and the time demands it. For his affair, peace be upon him, was not strange or remote; rather it was continuous according to custom, proceeding upon the course of widespread matters. And what is of this way, it is not fitting for the rational person to occupy himself with rejecting it at first blush. For this reason, God taught him to argue with those who deny his affair and those who turn away from reflecting on the correctness of what he brought, by His saying, the Most High: 'Say: I am not a novelty among the messengers' (46:9), and His saying, the Most High: 'And there was no nation but a warner had passed away in it' (35:24), and His saying, the Most High: 'And for every people there is a guide' (13:7), and His saying, the Most High: 'Then We sent Our messengers successively' (23:44), and His saying, the Most High: 'Has it been a wonder to people that We revealed to a man from among them: Warn the people?' (10:2). And his coming was in accordance with the time of need for him, because he was in a period of intermission of messengers and erosion of sovereignty and effacement of the signs of truth and its traces and obliteration of its emblem and covering, along with the customary practice of God in punishing the causes of guidance when the people of truth deviate from His path and stray from His way. And what is of this way, hastening to accept it is obligatory, and reason does not permit turning away from its like. For this reason God, the Most High, said: 'Indeed Our messenger has come to you making clear to you, after an interval of messengers' (5:19). And those addressed were in a place of need for him, because their kind was devoid of means of knowledge. For this reason He, the Most High, said: 'It is He who has sent among the unlettered a messenger from among themselves' (62:2). So his establishment was in the most apparent of places to creation, for it is the center of the kingdoms of the earth, and it is the meeting place of all the Arabs and the market of the people of distant horizons every year. And whatever appears of great matters and momentous news in such a place, its dissemination and the appearance of its traces are faster, and its reaching to those in the far ends of the earth and its regions is swifter and more rapid. And this is the most appropriate of the places of the earth and its regions for one who is sent to all people to be in it. God said: 'And thus We have revealed to you an Arabic Quran that you may warn the Mother of Cities and those around it' (42:7). Then his coming was after the people's wish for that and the manifestation of desire, according to what God, the Most High, said...
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a rational and scriptural argument for the necessity of accepting the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him) without initial rejection. It is a continuous argument consisting of three parts: first, a general principle about what kinds of claims should be rejected at first blush; second, an application showing that Muhammad's claim does not fall under that category; third, specific circumstances that make his coming timely and appropriate.

The author begins by stating his method: before proving the miracles and signs individually, he wants to establish a general rational principle that any fair-minded rational person can see. The principle is that a claim is rightly rejected immediately if it is impossible, or if it is so strange and unheard of that the time and place do not accept it, and there is no need for it. Since the claim of Muhammad's prophethood is none of these—it is not impossible, not strange in itself, given the continuous history of prophets before him—the rational person should hasten to accept it even with minimal evidence.

The author then supports this with several Quranic verses that show that Muhammad was not a novelty among messengers, that every nation had a warner, that messengers were sent consecutively, and that people should not be amazed at divine revelation to a man. These verses establish that prophethood is a customary divine practice.

Next, he argues that Muhammad's coming was at a time of great need: there was a period of intermission between prophets, signs of truth had faded, and the people were in need of guidance. God's custom is to send guidance when it is needed most. Therefore, reason itself requires acceptance, not rejection.

Finally, he points out the optimal location of his mission: Mecca, the center of the earth, the meeting place of all Arabs and international traders, ensuring rapid spread of his message. God chose the 'Mother of Cities' to warn all people. The passage ends with an incomplete statement about the people's desire for a prophet before his coming, but the argument is clear that everything about Muhammad's prophethood is rationally compelling and in line with divine custom.

Key Terms
أم القرى
رسالة
عاقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi argue that Mecca was the best place for the Prophet's mission?
A) Because it was the most populated city.
B) Because it was a center of trade and pilgrimage, ensuring fast spread.
C) Because it was the birthplace of the Prophet.
D) Because it was far from enemies.

Key Takeaway
Mecca's central location and annual gatherings made it the perfect place for the Prophet's message to reach the world quickly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever seen your own city, workplace, or social circle as a strategic location appointed by Allah for a purpose? If Mecca was chosen for its centrality and reach, where has Allah placed you to spread His message, and are you using that position intentionally?
?
The Prophet’s message spread quickly because it started in a place of gathering. Think of your own 'gathering points'—the places where people naturally come together in your life (family gatherings, friend groups, online spaces). Are you using them to share what you know of truth, or are you keeping it to yourself?
?
If the people of Mecca longed for a prophet before he came, what deep longings does your own soul have that only Allah can fulfill? Are you actively seeking that fulfillment, or have you become content without it?

Applying the Condition of Possibility to the Prophet's Claim
Nasafi examines whether the Prophet's claim meets the condition of possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi applies the condition of possibility to the Prophet's claim and the two aspects needed to examine its possibility.

Core Idea
The Prophet's claim is possible and must be examined scientifically and practically.

Nasafi continues his analysis of the prophet's claim by checking whether it meets the condition of possibility. He distinguishes two sub-claims and shows that the first is not only possible but obligatory to accept. The second requires examination from a scientific and practical perspective. This lesson guides you through this application.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Notice the structure of the argument.
The passage assumes prior knowledge of the 'five states' mentioned earlier.
2
Identify the two sub-claims
Find the two things the Prophet claimed according to the text. Note how the author treats the first as obligatory to accept.
The first sub-claim is about his being sent; the second is about the content he brought.
3
Understand the two aspects of examination
The second sub-claim's possibility is examined from the scientific and practical aspects. Locate these in the passage and see what the scientific aspect involves.
The scientific aspect looks at the principles of tawhid, creation, and resurrection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنه عليه السلام ادعى الرسالة من ربه إلى خلقه فبنا أن نتأمل أن هذه المعاني الأربعة هل وجدت أم لا؟ فنقول : أما دعواه فواقعة في حيز الإمكان فإنه ادعى أمرين: أحدهما أن الله تعالى أرسله إلى الخلق ليدعوهم إلى ما ارتضاه دينا لهم، وهذه في حيز الإمكان بل في حيز وجوب القبول لما اقترن به من الأحوال الخمسة التي مر ذكرها. والثاني: أن ما أتى به هو الذي ارتضاه الله تعالى لعباده، وهو ممكن، ومعرفة إمكانها متعلق باستبراء ذلك من وجهين: أحدهما: الوجه الأول العلمي. والثاني: الوجه العملي. فأما الوجه العلمي فبأن ننظر إلى ما يدعو الخلق إليه من أصول التوحيد وأصول بدء الخلق وإعادته، وقد وجد ذلك بحمد الله على ما تقتضيه العقول
Then indeed, peace be upon him, he claimed prophethood from his Lord to His creation, so it is upon us to reflect whether these four meanings are found or not. So we say: As for his claim, it falls within the realm of possibility, for he claimed two things: one, that God Almighty sent him to creation to call them to what He approved as a religion for them, and this is in the realm of possibility, rather in the realm of obligatory acceptance because of the five states that have been mentioned. And the second, that what he brought is what God Almighty approved for His servants, and it is possible, and knowledge of its possibility depends on examining it from two aspects: one is the scientific aspect, and the second is the practical aspect. As for the scientific aspect, it is that we look at what he calls creation to from the principles of tawhid and the principles of the beginning of creation and its return, and that has been found, praise be to God, in accordance with what the intellects require.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage applies the four previously mentioned conditions to the claim of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). The author first states that we must reflect whether these conditions are met. He then focuses on the first condition, possibility, and explains that the Prophet's claim actually consists of two sub-claims: (1) that God sent him to call people to a religion, and (2) that the content he brought is exactly what God approved. Both are possible. The author asserts that the first sub-claim is not only possible but even obligatory to accept because of the five states (أحوال خمسة) mentioned earlier (likely in previous pages). For the second sub-claim, its possibility is to be examined from two angles: the scientific (العلمي) and the practical (العملي). The author then begins explaining the scientific aspect: it involves examining the doctrines the Prophet calls to, namely the principles of tawhid (monotheism), the origins of creation, and the return (resurrection). He states that these teachings align with what sound intellects demand, thus establishing their possibility from a rational perspective.

Correction: The passage references the five states as previously mentioned, but does not elaborate on them here. They are assumed from earlier discussion.

C) Because it is rationally self-evident.

Key Takeaway
The condition of possibility is applied to the Prophet's claim by distinguishing two sub-claims and requiring both scientific and practical examination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
